The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Valid Conjunctions with Universal Affirmative Categoricals
Author enumerates the first four valid conjunctions with universal affirmative categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four valid conjunctive syllogisms (with universal affirmative categoricals) is represented by a given combination of premises.

Core Idea
When the categoricals are universal affirmative, the disjunct's quantity and quality determine the conclusion.

This passage from Daqā'iq al-Ḥaqā'iq presents the foundational forms of conjunctive syllogisms where a disjunctive major premise is paired with two universal affirmative categoricals. The author systematically varies the quantity and quality of the parts of the disjunction, showing how the conclusion changes. By mastering these four patterns, you lay the groundwork for understanding all subsequent variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
Locate the proposition that begins with 'دائماً إما... وإما' (always either...or). Note the two parts: they are the two options. In the first conjunction, both parts are universal affirmatives: 'كل ب ج' and 'كل ب د'.
The Arabic variables (ب, ج, د) represent terms; think of them as placeholders.
2
Examine the categorical minor premises
The minors are 'كل ج أ' and 'كل د أ' (Every J is A, Every D is A). In the first four conjunctions, both categoricals are universal affirmative. This is constant.
3
Analyze the disjuncts' quantity and quality
For each conjunction, note how the parts of the disjunction change: universal affirmative (first), particular negative (second), particular affirmative (third), universal negative (fourth). This is the only variable.
The text marks each change explicitly.
4
Trace the reasoning to the conclusion
Follow the author's reasoning: in the second conjunction, if 'Not every B is J' is true, then 'Every B is D' must be true (due to the disjunction), and since 'Every D is A', we get 'Every B is A'. Similarly for other cases. Verify that the conclusion matches the description given.
The fourth conjunction uses conversion: from 'No B is J' we infer 'Some B is D' (by obversion? actually it's immediate inference).
5
Summarize the pattern
When both categoricals are universal affirmative, the conclusion is universal affirmative only when the disjuncts are universal; it is particular affirmative when disjuncts are particular or universal negative.
This pattern emerges from the logical equivalences underlying the disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
والحمْليات كليات، موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(كل "ج" "أ") و(كل "د" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئيات سوالب، ونتيجته كالتي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق (ليس كل "ب" "ج") صدق (كل "ب" "د") وكان (كل "د" "أ") وإن صدق (ليس كل "ب" "د") صدق (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "د" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة، ونتيجته حَمْلية، وهي (بعض "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال كليات سوالب، ونتيجته كالتي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") صدق (بعض "ب" "د") وإن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "د") صدق (بعض "ب" "ج").
وإن كانت الحمليات كليات سوالب فقد تأتلف منها مع المنفصلات الكلية الموجبة اقترانات آخر أربعة على وزان هذه الاقترانات إلا أن النتيجة فيها حَمْلية سالبة.
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أُخَر ثمانية على وزان الثمانية السابقة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
وإن كانت نسبة المحمول في الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، فشرط الإنتاج على ما سبق إذا كان المحمول واحداً متحد الكيفية، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية، إلا أن النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا منفصلة موجبة موضوع أجزاء الانفصال فيها هو موضوع أجزاء الانفصال في المنفصلة، ومحمولها إيجاب محمول الحمليات وسلبه. الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليات موجبة وحَمْليات كليّة مختلفة الكيفية كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(كل "ج" "أ") ولا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته (دائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ") لأنه إن صدق (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ") وإن صدق (كل "ب" "د") وكان (لا شيء من "د" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") واعتبر الباقي بنفسك.
Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.
And the categoricals are universal, affirmative, as our saying: (Always either every B is J or every B is D) and (Every J is A) and (Every D is A), therefore (Every B is A).
The second conjunction: The conjunction is the same, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if (Not every B is J) is true, then (Every B is D) is true, and (Every D is A) was [true], and if (Not every B is D) is true, then (Every B is J) is true, and (Every D is A) was [true], therefore (Every B is A).
The third conjunction: The conjunction is the same, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, and its conclusion is categorical, which is (Some B is A).
The fourth conjunction: The conjunction is the same, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if (No B is J) is true, then (Some B is D) is true, and if (No B is D) is true, then (Some B is J) is true.
And if the categoricals are universal negative, then there are four other conjunctions formed from them with the universal affirmative disjunctives, parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a negative categorical.
And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then eight other conjunctions are formed from it with the categoricals, parallel to the previous eight, so consider them yourself.
And if the predicate in the categoricals differs in quality, then the condition for production is as before, when the predicate is one and unified in quality, and its productive conjunctions are eight, except that the conclusion in them is only an affirmative disjunctive whose subject of the parts of the disjunction is the subject of the parts of the disjunction in the disjunctive, and its predicate is the affirmation and negation of the predicate of the categoricals. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and categoricals that differ in quality, as our saying: (Always either every B is J or every B is D) and (Every J is A) and (No D is A), and its conclusion is (Always either every B is A or no B is A); because if (Every B is J) is true and (Every J is A) was [true], then (Every B is A); and if (Every B is D) is true and (No D is A) was [true], then (No B is A). And consider the rest yourself.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of valid conjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-iqtiraniyya) that combine a disjunctive major premise (al-munfasila) with one or more categorical minor premises (al-hamliyya). The author begins with the simplest case: a universal affirmative disjunctive ('Always either every B is J or every B is D') with two universal affirmative categoricals ('Every J is A' and 'Every D is A'), yielding the universal affirmative conclusion 'Every B is A'. The reasoning is straightforward: if B is always either J or D, and both J and D are A, then B must be A.

Then four variations are given based on the quantity and quality of the disjuncts:
- Second: The disjuncts are particular negatives ('Not every B is J' etc.). The conclusion remains universal affirmative because if the disjunction is true, one disjunct must hold, and the corresponding categorical gives the conclusion.
- Third: The disjuncts are particular affirmatives ('Some B is J' etc.), leading to a particular affirmative conclusion ('Some B is A').
- Fourth: The disjuncts are universal negatives ('No B is J' etc.), also leading to a particular affirmative conclusion (by conversion).

The author then notes that if the categoricals are universal negative instead of affirmative, four parallel conjunctions yield negative categorical conclusions.

Next, if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, eight more conjunctions can be formed (parallel to the eight already considered: the four with universal affirmative categoricals plus the four with universal negative categoricals), left for the reader to derive.

Finally, the author treats the case where the two categoricals differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Under the condition that the predicate in the categoricals is the same term ('one and unified in quality'), eight productive conjunctions exist. The conclusion is always a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are the same subject and the contradictory predicates. An example is given: with 'Always either every B is J or every B is D', plus 'Every J is A' and 'No D is A', the conclusion is 'Always either every B is A or no B is A'. The reasoning is provided: if B is J then it is A, if B is D then it is not A.

The style is systematic, using the familiar variable notation (ب, ج, د, أ) as terms. The reader is encouraged to work out the remaining conjunctions ('consider the rest yourself'), reflecting the pedagogical purpose of the text.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحملية
الاقتران
النتيجة
جزئيات سوالب
جزئيات موجبة
كليات سوالب

Checkpoint
In the third conjunction, what is the conclusion?
A) كل ب أ
B) بعض ب أ
C) لا شيء من ب أ
D) دائماً إما كل ب أ وإما لا شيء من ب أ

Key Takeaway
With universal affirmative categoricals, the conclusion is universal affirmative only when the disjuncts are universal, and particular affirmative when they are particular or universal negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Instantiate the first conjunction using the following terms from kalām: B = 'the world', J = 'temporally originated', D = 'eternal', A = 'has a cause'. Write the premises and conclusion in Arabic (or transliterated) and identify the conjunction number.
?
Given the premises: 'Always either every action is obligatory or every action is permissible' and 'Every obligatory action is commanded' and 'Every permissible action is not commanded', determine which conjunction from the lesson (first through fourth) this corresponds to, and state the conclusion.

The Syllogism
advanced

Expanding the Conjunctions: Negative Categoricals, Particular Disjunctive, and Mixed Quality
Author extends the enumeration to negative categoricals, particular disjunctive, and mixed quality categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the valid conjunctions when the categoricals are universal negative, when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, and when the categoricals differ in quality.

Core Idea
Variations in quantity and quality of premises yield four, eight, and eight further valid conjunctions.

Building on the first four conjunctions, the author now extends the analysis to three new scenarios: categoricals that are universal negative, a disjunctive that is particular affirmative, and categoricals that differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Each scenario generates parallel patterns, and the author provides one example for the last case, leaving the rest for the reader to derive.

Guided Path
1
Scenario A: Universal negative categoricals
When the categorical minors are both universal negative (e.g., 'لا شيء من ج أ' and 'لا شيء من د أ'), the author says there are four conjunctions parallel to the first four, but the conclusions are negative categoricals. Trace the reasoning: if the disjunction is universal affirmative and both categoricals are universal negative, what conclusion follows?
The author does not give explicit proofs for these, but the pattern is symmetric.
2
Scenario B: Particular affirmative disjunctive
If the disjunctive is particular affirmative ('بعض الأوقات إما... وإما'), then eight conjunctions are formed by combining with universal affirmative and universal negative categoricals. The author says 'consider them yourself,' so you need to derive them by analogy with the first eight.
The eight come from: 4 with universal affirmative categoricals (like first four) plus 4 with universal negative categoricals.
3
Scenario C: Categoricals differing in quality
When one categorical is affirmative and the other negative, with the same predicate term, the conclusion becomes an affirmative disjunctive. The author gives one example: universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative and universal negative categoricals yields a universal affirmative disjunctive conclusion.
The condition is that the predicate in both categoricals must be the same term (here A).
4
Analyze the provided example for Scenario C
Given 'Always either every B is J or every B is D', 'Every J is A', and 'No D is A', the conclusion is 'Always either every B is A or no B is A'. Verify: if B is J, then B is A (from first categorical); if B is D, then B is not A (from second categorical). Since B must be either J or D, it follows that either all B are A or no B is A.
This is a valid but non-constructive conclusion; it leaves the matter undetermined.
5
Generalize to remaining seven conjunctions of Scenario C
The author says there are eight productive conjunctions in this case. By varying the quantity and quality of the disjuncts (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) while keeping one categorical affirmative and one negative, you can generate them. The conclusion will always be an affirmative disjunctive with the same subject and contradictory predicates.
The condition 'when the predicate is one and unified in quality' means the two categoricals have the same predicate term but opposite qualities.

Source Text

Three Base Conjunctions with Multiple Predicates in Categoricals
Author presents three base conjunctions with multiple categorical predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three base conjunctions and construct their conclusions when the disjunctive is universal affirmative and the categoricals have multiple predicates.

Core Idea
When categorical predicates differ, the conclusion is a non-exclusive disjunction that allows both true.

In the previous lesson, we examined conjunctions where the categorical premises share a single predicate. Now, the author extends the analysis to cases where the categoricals have different predicates (here labeled 'A' and 'R'). He presents three base conjunctions, all with a universal affirmative disjunctive major, and shows that the conclusion is always an affirmative disjunction of the type man'iat al-khuluw (inclusive or). Understanding these base patterns is essential for mastering the more complex variations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three base conjunctions in the Arabic
Find the sentences starting with الاقتران الأول، الثاني، الثالث. They are clearly marked and each includes a disjunctive premise, two categorical premises, and a conclusion.
2
Extract the logical form using variables
Note the variables: B is the subject of the disjunctive, J and D are the two predicates in the disjuncts, A and R are the predicates in the categoricals. In the first conjunction: disjunctive 'either every B is J or every B is D', categoricals 'every J is A' and 'every D is R'.
The Arabic uses 'أ' and 'ر' for the two categorical predicates; here we use A and R for clarity.
3
Check the conclusion pattern
The conclusion for the first conjunction is 'always it is not empty that either every B is A or every B is R'. This is man'iat al-khuluw: it excludes only the case where both disjuncts are false.
The same pattern holds for the second and third conjunctions, with adjustments for negative or mixed categoricals.
4
Note the commonality
In all three base conjunctions, the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction. The quality of the categoricals (affirmative or negative) determines the quality of the corresponding part in the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول في الحمليات متعدداً فالشرط في الإنتاج على ما سبق، إذا كان المحمول في الحَمْليات واحداً، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والنتيجة في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع:
*
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(كل "ج" "أ") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو أن يكون إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "ب" "ر").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") و(لا شيء من "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "ر").
الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ر" وإما لا شيء من "ج" "أ").
وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال في المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد تتركب منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ثلاثة موازية لهذه الاقترانات.
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تتركب منها مع الحمليات اقترانات آخر ثلاثة موازية لها أيضاً غير أن النتيجة فيها تكون منفصلة موجبة، أجزاؤها جزئية.
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب تركب منها أيضاً مع الحمليـات اقترانات ثلاثة كما قبلها.
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تركب منها مع الحَمْليات اثنا عشر اقتراناً أخر موازية للاثني عشر المتقدمة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, then the condition for production is as before, when the predicate in the categoricals is one, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunction, exclusive in terms of emptiness (man'iat al-khuluw) without conjunction (without jam'):
* 
The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal categoricals affirmative, as our saying: (Always either every B is J or every B is D) and (Every J is A) and (Every D is R), and its conclusion is (Always it is not empty that either every B is A or every B is R).
The second conjunction: The conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as our saying: (Always either every B is J or every B is D) and (No J is A) and (No D is R), and its conclusion is (Always it is not empty that either no B is A or no B is R).
The third conjunction: The conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals differ in quality, as our saying: (Always either every B is J or every B is D) and (No J is A) and (Every D is R), and its conclusion is (Always it is not empty that either every B is R or no J is A).
And if the parts of the disjunction in the disjunctive are particular negatives, then three other conjunctions are composed from them with the categoricals, parallel to these conjunctions.
And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, then three other conjunctions are composed from them with the categoricals, parallel to them as well, except that the conclusion in them is an affirmative disjunction whose parts are particular.
And if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, three conjunctions are also composed from them with the categoricals, as before them.
And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, twelve other conjunctions are composed from them with the categoricals, parallel to the previous twelve, except that the conclusion in them is only a particular affirmative disjunction, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis to cases where the categorical premises have different predicates (here labeled 'A' and 'R', but originally 'أ' and another term; the OCR shows 'ر'). The author states that if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, the same condition applies as when the predicate is one, but now the number of productive conjunctions is twenty-four, and the conclusion is always an affirmative disjunction that is exclusive in terms of emptiness (man'iat al-khuluw), meaning that it is not possible for both disjuncts to be false, but they could both be true (contrary to a strict exclusive disjunction).

Three initial conjunctions are given, all with a universal affirmative disjunctive major:
- First: Both categoricals are universal affirmative ('Every J is A', 'Every D is R'). Conclusion: 'Always it is not empty that either every B is A or every B is R' – meaning B is either A or R, possibly both.
- Second: Both categoricals are universal negative ('No J is A', 'No D is R'). Conclusion: 'Always it is not empty that either no B is A or no B is R' – B is either not A or not R, possibly not both.
- Third: Categoricals differ in quality ('No J is A', 'Every D is R'). Conclusion: 'Always it is not empty that either every B is R or no J is A' – somewhat asymmetrical; the conclusion ties the subject B to R if B is D, and ties J to A via negation.

Then variations based on the quantity and quality of the disjuncts in the disjunctive premise are given:
- If the disjuncts are particular negatives, three more conjunctions parallel to the above.
- If the disjuncts are particular affirmatives, three more with particular disjunctive conclusions.
- If the disjuncts are universal negatives, another three.
- Finally, if the disjuncts are particular affirmatives, twelve more conjunctions parallel to the previous twelve (i.e., the 12 from the earlier three sets of three?), but with particular affirmative disjunctive conclusions.

The reader is again left to work out the details, indicating that the text is meant to guide the student through a systematic classification rather than exhaustively listing every form.

A) Always it is not empty that either every B is A or every B is R.
B) Always every B is A and every B is R.
C) Always it is not false that either every B is A or every B is R.
D) Always it is not empty that either no B is A or no B is R.

Key Takeaway
When categorical premises have multiple predicates and the disjunctive is universal affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction of the same inclusive type.

Variations in Disjunct Types for Conjunctions with Multiple Predicates
Author details variations in disjunct quantity and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number and type of conjunctions generated when the disjuncts vary in quantity and quality (particular negatives, particular affirmatives, universal negatives).

Core Idea
The three base patterns repeat for each disjunct type, but the conclusion's quantity matches that of the disjuncts.

After establishing the three base conjunctions with a universal affirmative disjunctive, the author now considers cases where the disjuncts themselves vary. He examines four scenarios: disjuncts that are particular negatives, particular affirmatives, universal negatives, and again particular affirmatives (with twelve conjunctions). The pattern is systematic: for each type, there are three conjunctions parallel to the base ones, except the conclusion takes the quantity of the disjuncts. Mastering this variation is key to handling all possible mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four variations enumerated by the author
In the Arabic, look for the phrases 'وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال في المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب' (particular negatives), 'وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة' (particular affirmatives), 'وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب' (universal negatives), and then again 'وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة' (particular affirmatives, but now with twelve conjunctions).
The last variation is actually a second case of particular affirmative disjuncts, but the author distinguishes it from the earlier three as generating twelve conjunctions.
2
For each variation, note the number of conjunctions
The author says: for particular negatives: three; for particular affirmatives: three; for universal negatives: three; for particular affirmatives (again): twelve. Combined with the three base conjunctions, that totals twenty-four productive conjunctions.
3
Understand the parallel structure
Each variation parallels the three base conjunctions. For example, the three conjunctions with particular negative disjuncts correspond to the three base ones but with the disjuncts changed from universal to particular negative. The categorical premises remain the same type (affirmative, negative, or mixed) as in the base patterns.
4
Check the conclusion type
For each variation, the conclusion is an affirmative disjunction of the inclusive type (man'iat al-khuluw), but its quantity matches the quantity of the disjuncts. For particular disjuncts, the conclusion is particular; for universal disjuncts, the conclusion is universal. The quality (affirmative or negative) of the conclusion mirrors the quality of the disjuncts.
The author explicitly states for particular affirmative disjuncts: 'the conclusion in them is an affirmative disjunction whose parts are particular.' The same logic applies to other variations.

Enumeration of 24 Disjunctive Syllogisms in First Figure
Author enumerates 24 productive conjunctions for first figure with multiple categoricals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 24 productive conjunctions for first figure disjunctive syllogisms with multiple categoricals and generate the conclusion for any such conjunction.

Core Idea
In first figure disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion is always an exclusive disjunction formed from the categorical predicates.

This passage systematically lists all valid syllogistic forms that combine an exclusive disjunction with two categorical premises in the first figure. Understanding these 24 conjunctions allows you to reason in complex legal and theological scenarios where a disjunctive ruling is further specified by conditional rulings. The author gives three concrete examples and then outlines the pattern for the remaining 21.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the syllogism
Each conjunction consists of one disjunctive major premise (exclusive type, man'at al-khuluw) with two parts, and two categorical premises. The disjunctive parts share the same subject (e.g., B) and have different predicates (e.g., J and D). The categoricals assign new predicates (e.g., A and R) to those disjunctive predicates.
The disjunction is always exclusive: the two parts cannot both be false, but may both be true.
2
Analyze the first example conjunction
For universal affirmative disjunctive 'Always either every B is J or every B is D', and universal affirmative categoricals 'Every J is A' and 'Every D is R', the conclusion is 'Always either every B is A or every B is R' – a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction.
The subject (B) remains the same; the predicates (A and R) come from the categoricals.
3
Follow the variations in the next two examples
The second example uses universal negative categoricals, yielding a universal negative disjunctive conclusion. The third example mixes one negative and one affirmative categorical, producing a mixed-quality disjunctive conclusion. This shows how the quality of the conclusion depends on the categoricals.
The conclusion always preserves the exclusive disjunction form (man'at al-khuluw).
4
Recognize the pattern for the remaining conjunctions
The disjunctive parts can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. For each type, three sub-conjunctions arise based on the categoricals (both affirmative, both negative, one affirmative one negative). This yields 4×3=12 conjunctions for universal parts and 12 for particular parts, totaling 24.
The author explicitly says 'consider the rest yourself', indicating the pattern is fully described.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول في الحمليات متعدداً فالشرط في الإنتاج على ما سبق، إذا كان المحمول في الحَمْليات واحداً، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والنتيجة في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(كل "ج" "أ") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو أن يكون إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "ب" "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") و(لا شيء من "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "ر"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ر" وإما لا شيء من "ج" "أ"). وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال في المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد تتركب منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ثلاثة موازية لهذه الاقترانات. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تتركب منها مع الحمليات اقترانات آخر ثلاثة موازية لها أيضاً غير أن النتيجة فيها تكون منفصلة موجبة، أجزاؤها جزئية. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب تركب منها أيضاً مع الحمليـات اقترانات ثلاثة كما قبلها. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تركب منها مع الحَمْليات اثنا عشر اقتراناً أخر موازية للاثني عشر المتقدمة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, then the condition for production is as previously, when the predicate in the categoricals is one, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive (man'at al-khuluw) without conjunction (without jam'). The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal affirmative categoricals, as we say: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D, and every J is A, and every D is R', and its conclusion: 'Always it cannot be that it is not either every B is A or every B is R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as we say: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D, and nothing of J is A, and nothing of D is R', and its conclusion: 'Always it cannot be that it is not either nothing of B is A or nothing of B is R'. The third conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are different in quality, as we say: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D, and nothing of J is A, and every D is R', and its conclusion: 'Always it cannot be that it is not either every B is R or nothing of J is A'. And if the parts of the disjunction in the disjunctive are particular negatives, then from them with the categoricals are formed another three conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions. And if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, then from them with the categoricals are formed another three conjunctions parallel to them as well, but the conclusion in them is an affirmative disjunctive whose parts are particular. And if the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, from them also with the categoricals are formed three conjunctions as before. And if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, from them with the categoricals are formed twelve other conjunctions parallel to the previous twelve, except that the conclusion in them is only a particular affirmative disjunctive. So consider the rest yourself.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of the conditions for productive syllogisms in the first figure when the categorical premises have multiple predicates relative to the disjunctive premise. The author begins by stating that when the predicate in the categoricals is multiple—meaning there are multiple categorical premises, each with a distinct predicate—the conditions for producing a conclusion are the same as when the predicate is single. The productive conjunctions are twenty-four, and the conclusion in all cases is an affirmative disjunction of the exclusive type (man'at al-khuluw), meaning the two parts cannot both be false but may both be true. The author then gives the first three conjunctions as examples. The first conjunction has a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative categoricals, yielding a universal affirmative disjunctive conclusion. The second conjunction replaces the categoricals with universal negatives, yielding a universal negative disjunctive conclusion. The third conjunction mixes the categoricals (one negative, one affirmative), yielding a mixed quality disjunctive conclusion. After these examples, the author generalizes: if the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, three more parallel conjunctions exist; if particular affirmatives, three more; if universal negatives, three more; and if particular affirmatives, there are twelve more conjunctions parallel to the twelve already mentioned (since the previous twelve included the three from universal negative parts? Actually the text is dense: there are twelve from the earlier discussion of universal parts. So the total becomes 24). The author leaves the detailed enumeration to the reader ('consider the rest yourself'). Throughout, the conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction, but its quantity (universal or particular) matches the quantity of the disjunctive parts. This passage is strictly procedural, outlining the combinatorial possibilities for syllogisms in the first figure that combine a disjunctive major premise with multiple categorical premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of conclusion is produced in the third conjunction (mixed categoricals)?
A) A universal affirmative conditional
B) A universal affirmative disjunctive
C) A mixed-quality disjunctive: 'Always either every B is R or nothing of J is A'
D) A particular affirmative disjunctive

Key Takeaway
The 24 conjunctions show how to derive a valid exclusive disjunctive conclusion from an exclusive disjunctive major and two categorical minors in the first figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid first-figure disjunctive syllogism using the pattern of the second conjunction (disjunctive universal affirmative, categoricals universal negative) with Islamic subject matter. For example, use 'every adult' as subject, 'baligh' and 'ghayr baligh' as disjunctive predicates, and 'mukallaf' and 'ghayr mukallaf' as categorical predicates.
?
Given the following description: 'Either every sale is with consent or without consent. Every sale with consent is valid. Every sale without consent is invalid.' Identify which of the 24 conjunctions this corresponds to and write the conclusion.

Second Figure Disjunctive Syllogisms with Multiple Categoricals
Conditions for second figure disjunctive syllogisms with equal categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second-figure disjunctive syllogism with multiple categoricals and determine whether a conclusion follows.

Core Idea
Second-figure disjunctive syllogisms yield a conditional conclusion, not a direct categorical.

In this passage, al-Qazwini (or the author) extends the theory of disjunctive syllogisms to the second figure, where the categorical premises share a common structure. He specifies that the disjunction must be affirmative and exclusive (real or man'at al-khuluw), and the categoricals must be universal. The conclusion always takes the form of a conditional (muttasila). Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and kalam that use disjunctive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunction (munfasila) and note whether it is affirmative and of the exclusive type (real or man'at al-khuluw).
2
Identify the categorical premises
Find the categorical propositions (hamliyyat). Check that they are universal and share the same predicate (as stated), and their number equals the parts of the disjunction.
Due to OCR risk, the actual predicate may vary; rely on the condition 'al-mahmul fiha wahid'.
3
Check the conditions
Verify: disjunction affirmative and exclusive; categoricals universal. If these hold, the syllogism is productive.
4
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional (muttasila) whose antecedent is one part of the disjunction or its contradictory, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals.
The author gives examples: 'If every B is D then it is not the case that every B is A nor every B is R'.

Source Text

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. And if it is according to the second figure, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in them is one, and let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, then its condition for production is that the disjunction be affirmative, and that it be real or exclusive (man'at al-khuluw without jam'), and that the categoricals be universal, and its productive conjunctions are sixteen [conjunctions], and the conclusion in all is a conditional (muttasila) whose antecedent is one of the two parts of the disjunction or its contradictory, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives, and categoricals are universal affirmatives, as we say: 'Always either every B is J or D' and 'Every J is A' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: 'If every B is D then it is not the case that every B is A nor every B is R' due to their necessary subsumption under J which opposes D. The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as we say: 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion is like the previous one, namely: 'If every B is D then it is not the case that every B is A nor every B is R' because if 'not every B is J' contradicts 'not every B is D', then the contradictory of 'not every B is J' which is 'every B is J' contradicts the contradictory of 'not every B is D' which is 'every B is D'. So if 'not every B is D' is false, its contradictory is true, i.e., 'every B is D', and if 'every B is D' is true, then 'every B is J' is false, and A and R are subsumed under J, so it is impossible that 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' hold, otherwise 'every B is J' would be necessary, because it follows from
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of syllogisms with disjunctive premises, now turning to the second figure. The author first states the conditions: the disjunction must be affirmative and exclusive (real or man'at al-khuluw), the categoricals must be universal, and there are sixteen productive conjunctions. The conclusion is always a conditional (muttasila) where the antecedent is one part of the disjunction or its contradictory, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals. The author then gives an example of the first conjunction: a universal affirmative disjunction with universal affirmative categoricals, yielding the conditional 'If every B is D then it is not the case that every B is A nor every B is R'. The reasoning is that A and R are under J, which opposes D. The second conjunction changes the disjunctive parts to particular negatives, but the conclusion remains similar. The author provides a detailed logical justification: because 'not every B is J' and 'not every B is D' are contradictory? Actually the text explains that if 'not every B is D' is false, then its contradictory 'every B is D' is true; then 'every B is J' must be false (since the disjunction is exclusive?), and since A and R are under J, it follows that 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' cannot both be true. The passage cuts off mid-sentence in the explanation. This material is highly technical and assumes familiarity with the logical relations of contradiction and subsumption.

First Conjunction: Universal Affirmative Disjunction
First conjunction of second figure mixed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the first conjunction of the second figure mixed syllogism and verify its conclusion using the conditions.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative disjunction with universal categoricals forces a conditional conclusion.

This first conjunction demonstrates the basic pattern. Understanding its structure is key to grasping the other three variations. The author provides an example and a brief proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts
The disjunctive premise is 'Always either every B is J or D' (universal affirmative disjunction). The categoricals are 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' (universal affirmatives).
A and R are subjects of the categoricals; B is the subject of the disjunction.
2
State the conditions
The disjunction must be affirmative and true or exclusive (man'at al-khuluw). All categoricals must be universal.
These conditions are stated at the start of the passage for all four conjunctions.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R'.
The conclusion negates the subjects A and R from B when the second disjunct (D) is affirmed.
4
Understand the proof
Since A and R are inside J (because 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J'), and J is opposed to D, if B is D, B cannot be J, so B cannot be A or R.
The proof uses subordination and opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الثاني، والحَمْليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، والمحمول فيها واحد وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية فشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقترانا، والنتيجة في الكل متصلة موجبة المقدم منها أحد جزئي الانفصال أو نقيضه، والتالي نفي موضوعات الحمليات الاقتران الأول: من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّات موجبة، وحمليات موجبة كليّة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و (كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") ونتيجته إن كان كل "ب" "د" فلا يكون كل "ب" "أ" ولا "ر" ضرورة دخولهما تحت "ج" المعاند لـ "د". الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "ب" "د") و(كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") ونتيجته كالتي قبله وهي إن كان كل "ب" "د" فلا يكون كل "ب" "أ" ولا "ر" لأنه إذا كان ليس كل "ب" "ج" معانداً لقولنا: ليس كل "ب" "د" فنقيض ليس كل "ب" "ج" وهو (كل "ب" "ج") معاند لنقيض ليس كل "ب" "د" وهو (كل "ب" د") فإذا كان ليس كل "ب" "د" كاذباً، كان نقيضه حقاً، وهو (كل "ب" "د") وإذا كان (كل "ب" "د") حقاً كان (كل "ب" "ج") كذباً و"أ" و"ر" داخلان تحت "ج" فيمتنع أن يكون كل "ب" "أ" ولا "ر" وإلا كان (كل "ب" "ج") ضرورة أنه يلزم من وجود الأخص وجود والأعم، وذلك خلف. الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") ونتيجته إن كان (بعض "ب" "د" فلا يكون بعض "أ" ولا "ر" لأن كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") فلو كان (بعض "ب" "أ" أو "ر" لكان (بعض "ب" "ج") مع فرض (بعض "ب" "د") وهو خلف. الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال كليات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د" و (كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج" ونتيجته كالتي قبله وهي أنه إن كان (بعض "ب" "د") فلا يكون (بعض "ب" "أ" ولا "ر" لأنه لو كان بعض "ب" "أ" أو "ر") لكان (بعض "ب" "ج") ضرورة أن كل "أ" و "ر" "ج"، ويلزم منه كذب قولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و (لا شيء من "ب" "د") وهو خلف.
And as for if it is in the mode of the second figure, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in them is one, and let the assumption be if the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, then its condition for production is that the disjunction be affirmative, and that it be true or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universal. Its productive moods are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is one of the two parts of the disjunction or its negation, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives, and categoricals are universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either every B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, necessarily because both enter under J which is opposed to D. The second conjunction: same as the first, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion is like the previous, namely: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, because if 'Not every B is J' is opposed to our saying: 'Not every B is D', then the negation of 'Not every B is J', which is 'Every B is J', is opposed to the negation of 'Not every B is D', which is 'Every B is D'. So if 'Not every B is D' is false, its negation is true, i.e., 'Every B is D'. And if 'Every B is D' is true, then 'Every B is J' is false, and A and R enter under J, so it is impossible that every B is A and nor R, otherwise 'Every B is J' would necessarily follow because the existence of the more specific implies the existence of the more general, and that is a contradiction. The third conjunction: same, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either some B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R, because every A is J and every R is J, so if some B were A or R, then some B would be J given the assumption of some B is D, which is a contradiction. The fourth conjunction: same, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either no B is J or no B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R, because if some B were A or R, then some B would be J, making 'No B is J' and 'No B is D' false, and that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and examples for four types of mixed syllogisms under the second figure. The author first states the conditions: the disjunctive premise must be affirmative and true or exclusively disjunctive, and all categoricals must be universal. Then he lists sixteen productive moods, but only details four conjunctions. Each conjunction varies the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts—universal affirmative, particular negative, particular affirmative, universal negative—while keeping the categoricals universal affirmative. The conclusion always takes the form of a conditional: if one disjunct holds (or its negation), then the subjects of the categoricals are negated. The proofs rely on the opposition between disjuncts and the subordination of terms (e.g., A and R under J). The reasoning uses contradiction (khalf) to demonstrate validity.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
المنفصلة الحقيقية
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R.
B) If some B is D, then some B is A or R.
C) If every B is J, then every B is D.
D) If no B is J, then no B is D.

Key Takeaway
The first conjunction shows that a universal affirmative disjunction with universal categoricals forces a conditional conclusion that negates the subjects of the categoricals when one disjunct is affirmed.

Second Conjunction: Particular Negative Disjunction
Second conjunction of second figure mixed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second conjunction and explain why the conclusion is the same as in the first conjunction despite the different disjunction type.

Core Idea
Even with a particular negative disjunction, the conclusion mirrors the first conjunction due to the opposition of negations.

The second conjunction varies the disjunction to particular negatives, but the categoricals remain universal affirmatives. The author shows how the conclusion remains identical through a careful proof involving opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The disjunction is 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D' – a particular negative disjunction (both parts are particular negatives).
Not every B is J' means 'some B is not J' in classical logic.
2
Note the categoricals
The categoricals are still 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' (universal affirmatives).
The subjects A and R are the same as in the first conjunction.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is the same conditional: 'If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R'.
The conclusion is identical to the first conjunction.
4
Work through the proof
If 'not every B is D' is false, then 'every B is D' is true. Since 'not every B is J' is opposed to 'not every B is D', and the negation of 'not every B is J' is 'every B is J', the truth of 'every B is D' forces 'every B is J' to be false. But A and R are all J, so B cannot be A or R, otherwise B would be J.
The proof uses the opposition between the negations and the subordination of A and R under J.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for if it is in the mode of the second figure, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in them is one, and let the assumption be if the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, then its condition for production is that the disjunction be affirmative, and that it be true or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universal. Its productive moods are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is one of the two parts of the disjunction or its negation, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives, and categoricals are universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either every B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, necessarily because both enter under J which is opposed to D. The second conjunction: same as the first, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion is like the previous, namely: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, because if 'Not every B is J' is opposed to our saying: 'Not every B is D', then the negation of 'Not every B is J', which is 'Every B is J', is opposed to the negation of 'Not every B is D', which is 'Every B is D'. So if 'Not every B is D' is false, its negation is true, i.e., 'Every B is D'. And if 'Every B is D' is true, then 'Every B is J' is false, and A and R enter under J, so it is impossible that every B is A and nor R, otherwise 'Every B is J' would necessarily follow because the existence of the more specific implies the existence of the more general, and that is a contradiction. The third conjunction: same, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either some B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R, because every A is J and every R is J, so if some B were A or R, then some B would be J given the assumption of some B is D, which is a contradiction. The fourth conjunction: same, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either no B is J or no B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R, because if some B were A or R, then some B would be J, making 'No B is J' and 'No B is D' false, and that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and examples for four types of mixed syllogisms under the second figure. The author first states the conditions: the disjunctive premise must be affirmative and true or exclusively disjunctive, and all categoricals must be universal. Then he lists sixteen productive moods, but only details four conjunctions. Each conjunction varies the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts—universal affirmative, particular negative, particular affirmative, universal negative—while keeping the categoricals universal affirmative. The conclusion always takes the form of a conditional: if one disjunct holds (or its negation), then the subjects of the categoricals are negated. The proofs rely on the opposition between disjuncts and the subordination of terms (e.g., A and R under J). The reasoning uses contradiction (khalf) to demonstrate validity.

Key Terms

المنفصلة الحقيقية

A) If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R.
B) If some B is D, then some B is A or R.
C) If not every B is J, then not every B is D.
D) No conclusion can be drawn.

Key Takeaway
The second conjunction proves that even a particular negative disjunction can yield the same conditional conclusion as the first.

Third Conjunction: Particular Affirmative Disjunction
Third conjunction of second figure mixed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third conjunction and explain why the conclusion involves 'some' rather than 'every'.

Core Idea
With a particular affirmative disjunction, the conclusion becomes a particular negative conditional.

The third conjunction changes the disjunction to particular affirmatives. The categoricals remain universal affirmatives. The conclusion now involves 'some' instead of 'every', reflecting the weaker disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The disjunction is 'Always either some B is J or D' – a particular affirmative disjunction (first part is particular affirmative, second part is a term? Actually it's 'some B is J or D' meaning either some B is J or D; D is a term. The text says 'جزئية موجبة' so both parts are particular affirmatives? It says 'أجزاء الانفصال جزئية موجبة' so both parts are particular affirmatives. The example: 'دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د"' – 'some B is J or D' – that's one part particular affirmative. The second part is just 'D'? Actually it's 'إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د"' meaning 'either some B is J or D', but D is a term, so it's like 'some B is J or D' where D is a term? That seems odd. Re-reading the Arabic: 'دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د"' – the second part is 'د' (D) alone, not a full proposition. This likely means 'either some B is J or D is the case' but D is a term that stands for a property. Typically in logic, the second part would be 'some B is D'? But the text says 'وإما "د"' just D. However, in the translation, they kept 'or D'. In the context, it means the second disjunct is that B is D (but not quantified?). Actually the pattern from the first conjunction: 'إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د"' – 'either every B is J or D' – D is a term meaning 'every B is D'? No, it's 'إما د' meaning 'either D' which is shorthand for 'every B is D'? In the first conjunction, the translation says 'Always either every B is J or D' – it seems 'D' stands for 'every B is D'. Similarly here, 'some B is J or D' means 'either some B is J or some B is D'? The text is ambiguous. But the conclusion is: 'If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R'. So the second disjunct is about D. I think we should interpret as: the first disjunct is 'some B is J', the second disjunct is 'some B is D'? But the Arabic says 'وإما "د"' which is just 'or D'. Probably it's a shorthand for 'some B is D' as well. For consistency, in the logical_notation_panel we can treat it as 'some B is D'. The author likely means the same quantity for both parts. So we'll take the disjunction as 'either some B is J or some B is D' (both particular affirmatives).
The exact phrasing is 'إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د"' – 'either some B is J or D', but D is a term. In context, it implies the same quantifier.
2
Note the categoricals
The categoricals are 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' (universal affirmatives).
Same as previous conjunctions.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is: 'If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R'.
The conclusion now has 'some' instead of 'every'.
4
Understand the proof
If some B is D, and if some B were A or R, then that B would be J (since A and R are all J), which would contradict the assumption that some B is J and some B is D – because J and D are opposed? Actually the proof: since every A is J and every R is J, if some B is A or R, then some B is J. But we have 'some B is J' as part of the disjunction. The disjunction says either some B is J or some B is D (exclusive). So if some B is D, then some B is J must be false. So some B cannot be J, hence cannot be A or R.
The proof uses exclusivity of the disjunction.

Fourth Conjunction: Universal Negative Disjunction
Fourth conjunction of second figure mixed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth conjunction and explain the proof using universal negatives.

Core Idea
A universal negative disjunction with universal categoricals also yields a particular negative conditional conclusion.

The fourth conjunction uses a universal negative disjunction. The conclusion is the same as in the third conjunction, but the proof differs, relying on the falsity of both disjuncts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The disjunction is 'Always either no B is J or no B is D' – a universal negative disjunction (both parts are universal negatives).
'No B is J' means 'Every B is not J'.
2
Note the categoricals
The categoricals are still 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' (universal affirmatives).
Same as before.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is: 'If some B is D, then not some B is A nor R'.
Same as the third conjunction.
4
Work through the proof
If some B is D, then 'no B is D' is false, so the disjunction forces 'no B is J' to be false (since it's exclusive). Thus, some B is J. If some B were A or R, then that B would be J (since A and R are all J). But we already have some B is J, which is fine? Actually the proof says: if some B is A or R, then some B is J, but then both 'no B is J' and 'no B is D' would be false, which contradicts the disjunction. Wait: the disjunction says either no B is J or no B is D (exclusive). If some B is D, then 'no B is D' is false, so 'no B is J' must be true. But if some B is A or R, then some B is J, making 'no B is J' false. That would make both disjuncts false, contradicting the exclusive 'or'. Hence, some B cannot be A or R.
The proof uses the exclusivity and the fact that if some B is D, then 'no B is D' is false, forcing 'no B is J' true. But if some B is A/R, then 'no B is J' becomes false, leading to contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for if it is in the mode of the second figure, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in them is one, and let the assumption be if the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, then its condition for production is that the disjunction be affirmative, and that it be true or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universal. Its productive moods are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is one of the two parts of the disjunction or its negation, and the consequent is the negation of the subjects of the categoricals. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives, and categoricals are universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either every B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, necessarily because both enter under J which is opposed to D. The second conjunction: same as the first, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J' and its conclusion is like the previous, namely: If every B is D, then not every B is A nor R, because if 'Not every B is J' is opposed to our saying: 'Not every B is D', then the negation of 'Not every B is J', which is 'Every B is J', is opposed to the negation of 'Not every B is D', which is 'Every B is D'. So if 'Not every B is D' is false, its negation is true, i.e., 'Every B is D'. Mixed Syllogisms: Universal Negatives, Particular Disjunctives, and Multiple Predicates
Author discusses conditions for mixed syllogisms with negative categoricals, particular disjunctives, and multiple predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions and conclusions for mixed syllogisms with universal negative categoricals, particular disjunctives, and multiple predicates.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms with conditionals have regular patterns even when categoricals are negative or predicates differ.

In the previous lesson, we examined mixed syllogisms with universal affirmative categoricals and universal disjunctives. Now, the author extends the analysis to three more cases: when the categoricals are universal negatives, when the disjunction is particular, and when the categoricals have multiple predicates. You will learn the conditions for productivity and the resulting conclusion types.

Guided Path
1

Recall that mixed syllogisms with universal affirmative categoricals and universal disjunctives produce four moods. The author now extends this to other cases.
The base case is essential for understanding the extensions.
2

When categoricals are universal negatives, the same four-part structure applies, yielding four more moods. The pattern mirrors the affirmative case.
The author says 'four parts parallel to these four'.
3

If the disjunction is particular, there are eight moods, and the conclusion becomes a particular conjunctive. The author leaves the derivation to the student.
Particular disjunctives produce weaker conclusions.
4

When categoricals have different predicates, conditions include: the disjunction must be affirmative and exclusive (man'at al-khuluw), and the categoricals must be universal and uniform in quality. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conjunctive (if categoricals are affirmative) or a universal affirmative disjunctive (if negative).
Sixteen productive conjunctions are possible.

The author gives: 'Always either every B is J or D', 'Every A is J', 'Every R is D'. Conclusion: 'If every B is R, then not every B is A'. The reasoning uses the disjunction and the categoricals to derive the conditional.
The text cuts off, but the pattern is clear.

Third Conjunction: Disjunctive with Particular Affirmative Parts
Author explains the third conjunction with particular affirmative disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the validity of the third conjunction syllogism, which combines a disjunctive premise with particular affirmative parts and two universal categorical premises.

Core Idea
The third conjunction uses reductio to prove a conditional conclusion from a disjunction and categoricals.

In this lesson, we examine the third type of compound syllogism (iqtiran) where the disjunctive premise contains particular affirmative parts. The author demonstrates how the categorical premises interact with the disjunction to produce a valid conclusion via reductio. This form is essential for understanding how logical relations extend across multiple terms in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the disjunctive premise: 'Always, either some B is J or D', and the two categorical premises: 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J'.
The disjunctive parts are particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة).
2
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'If some B is D, then no A nor R is B.' Note that it is not a direct categorical conclusion but a hypothetical one.
This conditional follows from the premises by reductio.
3
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the opposite: some B is A or R. From the categoricals, some B would be J. But the disjunction says either some B is J or D. If some B is J and some B is D, both parts of the disjunction are true, which contradicts the intended exclusive meaning (or the proof assumes exclusivity).
The contradiction (خلف) shows the assumption is false, so the conditional holds.
4
Generalize the structure
This form can be adapted when the categoricals are universal negatives or when the disjunctive is particular. The author later mentions eight parallel forms.
The key is the reductio using the link B → J from the categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") ونتيجته إن كان (بعض "ب" "د") فلا يكون بعض "أ" ولا "ر" لأن كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") فلو كان (بعض "ب" "أ" أو "ر") لكان (بعض "ب" "ج") مع فرض (بعض "ب" "د") وهو خلف.

الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال كليات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د" و (كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "ج") ونتيجته كالتي قبله وهي أنه إن كان (بعض "ب" "د") فلا يكون (بعض "ب" "أ" ولا "ر") لأنه لو كان بعض "ب" "أ" أو "ر") لكان (بعض "ب" "ج") ضرورة أن كل "أ" و "ر" "ج"، ويلزم منه كذب قولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و (لا شيء من "ب" "د") وهو خلف.

وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت الحَمْليات كليّات سالبة، فإنها قد يتركب منها ومن المتصلة الكلية الموجبة أربعة أجزاء موازية لهذه الأربعة، وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية فقد يأتلف منها اقترانات أُخَر ثمانية على موازاة هذه الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون متصلة جزئية، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
The third conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, as in our statement: 'Always, either some B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J', and its conclusion is that if some B is D, then no A nor R is some B, because every A is J and every R is J, and if some B were A or R, then some B would be J while assuming some B is D, which is a contradiction.

The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as in our statement: 'Always, either nothing from B is J or nothing from B is D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J', and its conclusion is like the previous one, namely that if some B is D, then no B is A nor R, because if some B were A or R, then some B would be J necessarily since every A and R is J, and from that follows the falsehood of our statement 'nothing from B is J' and 'nothing from B is D', which is a contradiction.

And upon this, the production [of conclusions] is not hidden when the categoricals are universal negatives; for they may be combined with the universal affirmative conditional to form four parts parallel to these four. And if the disjunctive is particular, then other eight conjunctions may be composed parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a particular conditional, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage describes two specific forms of compound syllogisms (iqtiran) that combine a disjunctive premise (munfasila) with two categorical premises (hamliyyah). The first form, called the third conjunction, has a disjunctive with particular affirmative parts: 'Always, either some B is J or D'. Together with the categoricals 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J', the conclusion is that if some B is D, then no A or R is B. The proof is by reductio (khalaf): assuming the opposite leads to contradiction. The second form, the fourth conjunction, has a disjunctive with universal negative parts: 'Always, either nothing from B is J or nothing from B is D'. With the same categoricals, the conclusion is similar: if some B is D, then no B is A or R. The reductio here shows that otherwise both parts of the disjunction would be false. The author then generalises: if the categoricals are universal negatives instead of affirmatives, the same patterns yield four parallel valid forms. Additionally, if the disjunctive is particular (rather than universal), eight more forms arise, with the conclusion being a particular conditional. The reader is encouraged to work out the details. All examples use placeholder variables (A, B, J, D) representing terms in the Islamic logical tradition, where the logical structure is demonstrated abstractly.

Fourth Conjunction: Disjunctive with Universal Negative Parts
Author explains the fourth conjunction with universal negative disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the validity of the fourth conjunction syllogism, which combines a disjunctive premise with universal negative parts and two universal categorical premises.

Core Idea
The fourth conjunction uses reductio on a disjunction of universal negatives to prove that no B is A or R if some B is D.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth type of compound syllogism (iqtiran) where the disjunctive premise contains universal negative parts. The author demonstrates how this structure yields a similar conclusion to the third conjunction but with different quantifiers. This form is useful for arguments denying a predicate across multiple subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the disjunctive premise: 'Always, either nothing from B is J or nothing from B is D', and the two categorical premises: 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is J'.
The disjunctive parts are universal negatives (كليات سوالب).
2
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is: if some B is D, then no B is A nor R. This is similar to the third conjunction but derived from universal negative disjuncts.
The conclusion is stated as 'نتيجته كالتي قبله' (its conclusion is like the previous one).
3
Follow the reductio proof
Assume some B is A or R. Then from the categoricals, some B is J. But the disjunction says either nothing from B is J or nothing from B is D. If some B is J, then 'nothing from B is J' is false, so 'nothing from B is D' must be true. But if some B is D, then 'nothing from B is D' is also false. Thus both parts are false, contradicting the disjunction.
The contradiction shows the assumption leads to both disjuncts being false, impossible.
4
Generalize
This form also works when categoricals are universal negatives, and the disjunctive is particular, leading to eight parallel forms.
The author encourages the reader to work out the variations.

Source Text

Mixed Syllogisms with Multiple Predicates: Conditions and the First Form
Author gives conditions and first valid form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism with multiple predicates and trace the logical deduction of the conclusion in the first form.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism with multiple predicates requires a real disjunction and universal categoricals; its conclusion is a conditional.

In this lesson, we examine a more complex syllogistic form where the categorical premises share the same subject but have different predicates, and are combined with a disjunctive premise. The author of the Daqaiq al-Haqaiq lays out the necessary conditions and illustrates the first of sixteen valid forms. By understanding this, you will be able to recognize and construct such arguments in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Locate the disjunctive premise and the categorical premises in the example. Note that the categoricals share the same subject (B) but have different predicates (J and D).
The disjunctive is 'Always, either every B is J or D'.
2
Check the conditions
Verify that the disjunctive is affirmative and either real (haqiqiyya) or of the type 'mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'. Also check that the categoricals are universal and agree in quality (both affirmative).
The author requires these conditions for the syllogism to be productive.
3
Understand the specific property
Note that when the categoricals are affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional. When they are negative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive.
This property is specific to this type of mixed syllogism.
4
Trace the deduction
Follow the reductio argument: assume every B is R, then every B is D (since every R is D). Assume for contradiction that every B is A, then every B is J (since every A is J). This contradicts the exclusive disjunction (either J or D). Therefore, the assumption fails, yielding the conditional conclusion.
The conclusion is 'If every B is R, then not every B is A'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول في الحَمْليات متعدداً فشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية، أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحمليات كليات متفقة الكيفية، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه عند كون الحَمْليات موجبات متصلة موجبة، وعند كونها سوالب منفصلة موجبة: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّات موجبة، والحمليات موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د" و(كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ر" "د") ونتيجته إن كانت كل "ب" "ر" فلا يكون كل "ب" "أ" لأنه إذا كان كل "ب" "ر" فكل "ب" "د" ضرورة دخول كل "ر" تحت "د" ويلزم منه أن لا يكون كل "ب" "أ" لأنه لو كان كل "ب" "أ" لكان كل "ب" "ج" ضرورة دخول كل "أ" تحت "ج" وهو خلف.
And as for when the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, the condition for its production [of a conclusion] is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be real (haqiqiyya) or of the type 'preventive of emptiness without collectiveness' (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), and that the categoricals be universal and agreeing in quality. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions, and its specific property is that the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional when the categoricals are affirmatives, and a universal affirmative disjunctive when they are negatives. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives, and the categoricals are affirmatives, as in our statement: 'Always, either every B is J or D' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is D', and its conclusion is that if every B is R, then not every B is A, because if every B is R, then every B is D necessarily due to the inclusion of every R under D, and it follows that not every B is A, because if every B were A, then every B would be J necessarily due to the inclusion of every A under J, which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage addresses a more complex scenario where the categorical premises have multiple predicates—meaning they share the same subject but differ in predicates. The author states the conditions for a valid syllogism: the disjunctive premise must be affirmative and must be either 'real' (haqiqiyya, i.e., mutually exclusive and exhaustive) or of the type 'preventive of emptiness without collectiveness' (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam', meaning at least one of the two disjuncts is true, but both could be true). Additionally, the categoricals must be universal and of the same quality (all affirmative or all negative). Under these conditions, there are sixteen valid forms (qara'in). The specific property is that the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional when the categoricals are affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive when they are negative. The author then illustrates the first form: the disjunctive 'Always, either every B is J or D', with categoricals 'Every A is J' and 'Every R is D'. The conclusion is a conditional: 'If every B is R, then not every B is A'. The proof uses reductio: assuming every B is A leads to contradiction because then every B would be J (since every A is J) and also every B would be D (since every B is R and every R is D), which conflicts with the disjunction that requires either every B is J or D, but not both exclusively? Actually, the disjunction is likely exclusive (haqiqiyya) so both cannot be true. Hence the assumption fails. This shows the power of compound syllogisms in uncovering implications.

Key Terms
المحمول متعدد
منفصلة حقيقية
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
قرائن منتجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They must be particular and agreeing in quality.

Key Takeaway
The first form of mixed syllogism with multiple predicates shows how a disjunctive and categoricals yield a conditional conclusion through reductio.
