The Sixth Conjunction: Disjunctive with Particular Negative Parts
Author defines the sixth conjunction where the disjunctive parts are particular negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the sixth conjunction and derive its conclusion using the inference pattern described.

Core Idea
The sixth conjunction pairs a disjunctive of particular negatives with universal negatives to yield a new disjunction.

The sixth conjunction is a specific compound syllogism where the disjunctive premise has particular negative parts, and the categorical premises are universal negatives. The conclusion follows a pattern similar to previous conjunctions. Let's break down the components and see how the conclusion is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive premise is of the form 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D'. Note the quantifier 'always' indicates a necessary true disjunction (man'at al-khuluw).
The parts are particular negatives: 'not every B is C' is equivalent to 'some B is not C'.
2
Identify the categorical premises
There are two universal negative categorical premises: 'Nothing of A is C' and 'Nothing of R is D'. These are negations of the respective universal affirmatives.
The letters A, R are arbitrary placeholders for subjects; B is the subject of the disjunctive parts.
3
Understand the inference pattern
If 'not every B is C' is true, then it is false that every B is C. Combined with 'Nothing of A is C' (which implies A is a subset of non-C), it forces 'Every B is D' to be true. Similarly for the other side. This yields the conclusion: either every B is C or every B is D.
The inference relies on the exclusivity of the disjunction and the categoricals.
4
State the conclusion
The conclusion is a disjunction of the two universal affirmatives: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D'. The author says it is like the previous conjunction.
Thus the conclusion mirrors the disjunctive premise but with the negations removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب كقولنا: (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "ب" "د" ولا شيء من "أ" "ج") ولا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله، لأنه إن صدق (ليس كل "ب" "ج") صدق ( كل "ب" "د") وإن صدق ليس كل "ب" "د") صدق (كل "ب" "ج" ) والحكم كالحكم.
The sixth conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, as in our statement: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D, and nothing of A is C, and nothing of R is D.' Its conclusion is like the one before it, because if 'not every B is C' is true, then 'every B is D' is true, and if 'not every B is D' is true, then 'every B is C' is true. The ruling is the same as the ruling.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth conjunction in a series of compound syllogisms involving a disjunctive premise and two categorical premises. The disjunctive premise here has its parts as particular negative propositions (e.g., 'not every B is C' and 'not every B is D'). The categorical premises are universal negatives (e.g., 'nothing of A is C' and 'nothing of R is D'). The conclusion is a disjunction similar to previous conjunctions. The reasoning: if one part of the disjunctive is true, the other must be true, leading to the conclusion. In logical terms, this pattern uses immediate inferences between the universal negatives and the particular negatives to derive the conclusion.

Seventh Conjunction: Disjunctive with Positive Particular Parts
Definition of the seventh conjunction with particular positive disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the seventh conjunction (disjunctive with particular affirmative parts) and determine its conclusion.

Core Idea
Affirmative particulars in disjuncts yield negative particulars in the conclusion.

This lesson examines the seventh type of disjunctive syllogism in Islamic logic. Here, the disjunctive premise consists of two particular affirmative propositions, paired with universal negative categoricals. The conclusion is a disjunction of particular negatives. Mastering this structure sharpens your ability to evaluate complex arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive parts
Locate the two disjuncts in the disjunctive premise; they are particular affirmative propositions (e.g., 'some B is C' and 'some B is D').
These are affirmative because they state existence without quantity.
2
Identify the categorical premises
Find the universal negative premises: 'nothing of A is C' and 'nothing of R is D'.
These are universal negatives, asserting that no member of A is a member of C, and similarly for R and D.
3
Derive the conclusion pattern
From each disjunct combined with the corresponding categorical, derive a particular negative: if some B is C and no A is C, then some B is not A; similarly for D. The conclusion joins these with 'or'.
The conclusion is a disjunction of particular negatives.
4
Verify validity
The argument is a constructive dilemma: if P then Q, if R then S, and P or R, therefore Q or S. Here P='some B is C', Q='some B is not A', etc.
The reasoning is valid because each conditional is justified by the universal negative categorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران السابع: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "أ" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته (إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "ب" ليس "ر") لأنه إن صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإن صدق (بعض "ب" "د") وكان (لا شيء من "ر" "د") فـ (بعض "ب" ليس "ر").
The seventh conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, as in our statement: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D, and nothing of A is C, and nothing of R is D.' Its conclusion is 'Either some B is not A or some B is not R,' because if 'some B is C' is true and 'nothing of A is C' is true, then 'some B is not A'; and if 'some B is D' is true and 'nothing of R is D' is true, then 'some B is not R'.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage describes the seventh conjunction, where the disjunctive premise has particular affirmative parts (e.g., 'some B is C' and 'some B is D'). The categorical premises are universal negatives (e.g., 'nothing of A is C' and 'nothing of R is D'). The conclusion is a disjunction of two particular negatives: 'some B is not A or some B is not R'. The reasoning uses the fact that if some B is C, and no A is C, then that B is not A; similarly for D. The conclusion mirrors the structure of the disjunctive parts, but with a negative predicate due to the universal negative categoricals.

Eighth Conjunction: Disjunctive with Universal Negatives
Author defines the eighth conjunction with universal negative disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the eighth conjunction and explain why the contradictories of the disjunctive parts cannot both be true.

Core Idea
Universal negative disjunctives force their contradictories to also be exclusive.

In this lesson, we explore the eighth conjunction where the disjunctive premise consists of universal negatives. The author shows that because the two universal negatives are mutually exclusive (exactly one is true), their contradictories also cannot coexist. This subtle implication relies on subsumption relationships from the categorical premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the statement that starts with 'إما' and contains universal negatives. Here it is: 'دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د"'.
'كليات سوالب' means universal negative propositions.
2
Determine the disjunction type
Note that the disjunction is exclusive. The author says 'لا يجتمع', meaning the two parts cannot both be true. This implies exactly one is true.
An exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع والخلو) ensures that the two parts cannot both be true or both false.
3
Derive the impossibility of both contradictories
Since exactly one of the universal negatives is true, one is false. Their contradictories (particular affirmatives) mirror the truth values: if p is true, not-p is false; if q is false, not-q is true. Hence the contradictories cannot both be true.
The author states: 'فلا يجتمع نقيض كل واحد منهما مع نقيض الآخر'.
4
Apply the categorical premises
The categorical premises ('لا شيء من أ ج' and 'لا شيء من ر د') establish subsumption relationships. For example, because every R is under D (implied by the categorical), if some B is R then some B is D, which would conflict with the disjunction's implication.
This step shows how the premises lead to further restrictions, but be cautious of potential textual inconsistencies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثامن الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب كقولنا: (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د" ولا شيء من "أ" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله لأنه كما لا يجتمع (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ب" "د") فلا يجتمع نقيض كل واحد منهما مع نقيض الآخر، وهو (بعض "ب" "ج") و (بعض "ب" "د") فلو كان (بعض "ب" "ر") لكان (بعض "ب" "د") لدخول كل "ر" تحت د فلو كان (بعض "ب" "أ") لكان (بعض "ب" "ج") لدخول كل "أ" تحت "ج" وقد قيل لا يجتمعان.
The eighth conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, as in our statement: 'Always either nothing of B is C or nothing of B is D, and nothing of A is C, and nothing of R is D.' Its conclusion is like the one before it, because just as 'nothing of B is C' and 'nothing of B is D' cannot both be true, so too their contradictories ('some B is C' and 'some B is D') cannot both be true. So if some B is R, then some B is D, since every R is under D. And if some B is A, then some B is C, since every A is under C. And it is said they cannot both be true.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth conjunction, where the disjunctive premise has universal negative parts (e.g., 'no B is C' and 'no B is D'). The categorical premises are also universal negatives. The conclusion is similar to the previous one. The explanation uses the fact that the two universal negatives cannot be simultaneously true (since they are exclusive in a true disjunction). Consequently, their contradictories (particular affirmatives) also cannot both be true. The author then shows a subtle implication: if some B is R (and R is a subset of D, because the categorical says 'no R is D'?), actually the text says 'since every R is under D', which seems to assume an inclusion relationship, possibly from the categorical 'nothing of R is D' implying that R is a subset of the complement of D? This part may require careful parsing. The conclusion is that the contrary pair cannot be true, mirroring the previous conjunction.

نقيض

Conditions for Disjunctive Third Figure Syllogisms
Author lists conditions for valid third-figure disjunctive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions required for a valid disjunctive syllogism in the third figure and determine whether a given conjunction meets them.

Core Idea
The third figure with a real disjunctive premise requires uniformity, equality, and exclusive disjunction for a valid conclusion.

In classical Islamic logic, the third figure of syllogism can be combined with a disjunctive premise to produce a rich set of valid conjunctions. The author outlines strict conditions that must hold for the conclusion to follow. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating any such argument in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh or kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term (B) is the subject of both the disjunctive premise and every categorical premise. That is the hallmark of the third figure.
Here B is the subject of 'every B is C', 'every B is D', 'every B is A', etc.
2
Verify the disjunctive type
Ensure the disjunctive premise is 'real' (haqiqiyya) – truly exclusive – or at least exclusive of emptiness (mani'a al-khuluw duna al-jam').
A real disjunction means one part is true and the other false; it cannot be both true.
3
Check uniformity of parts
The parts of the disjunctive must agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative) and quantity (both universal or both particular).
Uniformity ensures the conclusion is not ambiguous.
4
Check the categoricals
The categorical premises must match the number of disjunctive parts, be uniform in quantity, and be universal if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives.
The categoricals must also have the same subject (B) and each have a distinct predicate (A, R, etc.).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الثالث والمنفصلة حقيقية، وأجزاؤها متفقة الكيفية والكمية، والحَمْليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، وهي متفقة الكمية، فالمحمول فيها لا بد أن يكون واحداً؛ إذ الموضوع فيها واحد، فلو كان المحمول واحداً لما كانت الحَمْليات متعددة، بل لا بد أن يكون المحمول فيها متعدداً، وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات إن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كلية المنفصلة وجزئيتها وكليّة أجزائها وجزئيتها، وإيجابها وسلبها، وإيجاب الحَمْليات وسلبها وكليتها وجزئيتها، ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة، أجزاؤها كليات موجبة، وكليات حملية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع جزئية الأجزاء، وهي (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "أ" و "ر") أي لا يخلو حال منهما؛ لأنه إن صدق (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ر") وإن صدق (كل "ب" "د") وكان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") فـ (بعض "د" "أ" و "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سالبة كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" د" ولا شيء من "ب" "أ") ولا شيء من "ب" "ر") ونتيجته لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ" ولا "ر") والبيان كالذي قبله. الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "ب" "أ") و (لا شيء من "ب" "ر") ونتيجته (إما بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" إما "أ" وإما ليس "ر").
And if it is in the pattern of the third figure, and the disjunctive is real, and its parts are uniform in quality and quantity, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction and uniform in quantity, then the predicate in them must be one—since the subject in them is one; for if the predicate were one, the categoricals would not be multiple. Rather, the predicate must be multiple. Its condition for production is the affirmation of the disjunctive, that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity of the disjunctive, the universality and particularity of its parts, their affirmation and negation, and the affirmation and negation of the categoricals and their universality and particularity, are thirty-six conjunctions. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive, its parts are universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative categoricals, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and every B is R.' Its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, with particular parts, namely: 'Always either some B is C or some D is A and R,' meaning that the situation does not lack either of them, because if 'every B is C' is true and 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' are true, then 'some C is A and R'; and if 'every B is D' is true and 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' are true, then 'some D is A and R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and nothing of B is A, and nothing of B is R.' Its conclusion is: 'It does not lack that either some C or some D is not A and not R.' The explanation is like the previous one. The third conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the categoricals differ in quality, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and nothing of B is R.' Its conclusion is: 'Either some C or some D either is A or is not R.'
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage introduces a major classification of conjunctions when the disjunctive premise is arranged as the third figure (i.e., the subject of the categoricals is the middle term). The conditions are strict: the disjunctive must be real (true exclusive disjunction), its parts uniform in quality and quantity, and the categoricals equal in number to the disjunctive parts. Under these conditions, 36 valid conjunctions are enumerated. The author then details the first three. In the first, the disjunctive is universal affirmative with universal affirmative parts, and the categoricals are universal affirmatives. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular parts, indicating that some instances of the terms are connected. The second replaces the categoricals with universal negatives, yielding a conclusion that at least one of the particular parts negates both A and R. The third mixes an affirmative and a negative categorical, resulting in a conclusion that combines affirmation and negation. This systematic enumeration demonstrates the logical rigor involved in deriving conclusions from compound premises.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
منفصلة حقيقية
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
الحملية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They must be particular affirmatives
B) They must be universal affirmatives
C) They must be universal negatives
D) They can be any quality or quantity

Key Takeaway
A valid third-figure disjunctive syllogism requires a real disjunction, uniform parts, and appropriate categoricals to yield a necessary conclusion.

First Three Conjunctions of the Third Figure Pattern
Author details the first three conjunctions with logical variable examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first three conjunctions of the third figure disjunctive pattern and explain how the conclusion is derived from the premises.

Core Idea
Varying the quality of the categoricals changes the conclusion from universal affirmative to negative or mixed, but the form remains a disjunction of particulars.

After laying down the conditions, the author illustrates the system with the first three of the 36 valid conjunctions. Each shows how a specific combination of disjunctive and categorical premises yields a unique conclusion. This lesson walks through each example, revealing the logical mechanics behind the derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
In each conjunction, the disjunctive is universal affirmative: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D'. Note that the parts are universal affirmatives.
The disjunctive must be real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness.
2
List the categorical premises
For the first conjunction, the categoricals are 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' (both affirmative). For the second, they are 'nothing of B is A' and 'nothing of B is R' (both negative). For the third, one is affirmative and one negative.
The number of categoricals equals the number of disjunctive parts (two here).
3
Derive the conclusion for the first conjunction
If 'every B is C' is true, then with 'every B is A' and 'every B is R', we get 'some C is A and R' (since B is non-empty, there is a B that is C, A, R). Similarly for D. Hence the conclusion: 'Always either some C is A and R or some D is A and R'.
The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular parts (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam').
4
Modify for the second and third conjunctions
In the second, the categoricals are negative, so the conclusion becomes a universal disjunction with particular negatives: 'Always either some C is not A and not R or some D is not A and not R'. In the third, mixing affirmative and negative yields a mixed particular conclusion.
The pattern is consistent: the conclusion always disjoins two particular propositions derived from the disjunctive parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الثالث والمنفصلة حقيقية، وأجزاؤها متفقة الكيفية والكمية، والحَمْليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، وهي متفقة الكمية، فالمحمول فيها لا بد أن يكون واحداً؛ إذ الموضوع فيها واحد، فلو كان المحمول واحداً لما كانت الحَمْليات متعددة، بل لا بد أن يكون المحمول فيها متعدداً، وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات إن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كلية المنفصلة وجزئيتها وكليّة أجزائها وجزئيتها، وإيجابها وسلبها، وإيجاب الحَمْليات وسلبها وكليتها وجزئيتها، ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة، أجزاؤها كليات موجبة، وكليات حملية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع جزئية الأجزاء، وهي (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "أ" و "ر") أي لا يخلو حال منهما؛ لأنه إن صدق (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ر") وإن صدق (كل "ب" "د") وكان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") فـ (بعض "د" "أ" و "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سالبة كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" د" ولا شيء من "ب" "أ") ولا شيء من "ب" "ر") ونتيجته لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ" ولا "ر") والبيان كالذي قبله. الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "ب" "أ") و (لا شيء من "ب" "ر") ونتيجته (إما بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" إما "أ" وإما ليس "ر").
And if it is in the pattern of the third figure, and the disjunctive is real, and its parts are uniform in quality and quantity, and the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction and uniform in quantity, then the predicate in them must be one—since the subject in them is one; for if the predicate were one, the categoricals would not be multiple. Rather, the predicate must be multiple. Its condition for production is the affirmation of the disjunctive, that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity of the disjunctive, the universality and particularity of its parts, their affirmation and negation, and the affirmation and negation of the categoricals and their universality and particularity, are thirty-six conjunctions. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive, its parts are universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative categoricals, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and every B is R.' Its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, with particular parts, namely: 'Always either some B is C or some D is A and R,' meaning that the situation does not lack either of them, because if 'every B is C' is true and 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' are true, then 'some C is A and R'; and if 'every B is D' is true and 'every B is A' and 'every B is R' are true, then 'some D is A and R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and nothing of B is A, and nothing of B is R.' Its conclusion is: 'It does not lack that either some C or some D is not A and not R.' The explanation is like the previous one. The third conjunction: the conjunction remains as before, except that the categoricals differ in quality, as in our statement: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and nothing of B is R.' Its conclusion is: 'Either some C or some D either is A or is not R.'
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage introduces a major classification of conjunctions when the disjunctive premise is arranged as the third figure (i.e., the subject of the categoricals is the middle term). The conditions are strict: the disjunctive must be real (true exclusive disjunction), its parts uniform in quality and quantity, and the categoricals equal in number to the disjunctive parts. Under these conditions, 36 valid conjunctions are enumerated. The author then details the first three. In the first, the disjunctive is universal affirmative with universal affirmative parts, and the categoricals are universal affirmatives. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular parts, indicating that some instances of the terms are connected. The second replaces the categoricals with universal negatives, yielding a conclusion that at least one of the particular parts negates both A and R. The third mixes an affirmative and a negative categorical, resulting in a conclusion that combines affirmation and negation. This systematic enumeration demonstrates the logical rigor involved in deriving conclusions from compound premises.

D) By contraposing the categorical premises

Key Takeaway
The first three conjunctions show how varying the categoricals' quality produces different but always valid conclusions in the form of a universal disjunction of particular propositions.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Conditions for Third Figure Compound Syllogisms
Author lists conditions for third figure compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions required for a productive third figure compound syllogism with a disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
Productivity requires the disjunctive to be real or exclusive and categoricals to match its parts.

This lesson introduces the foundational conditions for constructing valid compound syllogisms in the third figure when the major premise is a disjunctive proposition. Understanding these conditions is essential before exploring the specific conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Recognize that the passage specifies 'third figure' (shakl thalith), where the middle term is the subject in both premises.
The middle term in the examples is B.
2
Note the disjunctive type
The disjunctive must be 'real' (haqiqiyya) or 'exclusive of emptiness without conjunction' (mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam‘). This ensures at least one disjunct is true.
Both types allow the categoricals to apply.
3
Check the categoricals
The categorical premises must equal the number of disjunctive parts and agree in quantity. If disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, categoricals must be universal; if parts are particular negatives, categoricals must be universal negatives.
This ensures valid inference in the third figure.
4
Confirm quality of disjunctive
The disjunctive must be affirmative (positive) for the combination to be productive.
Negation would change the logical form.

الانفصال، في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة إنما تكون من محمولات الحملية، وما خرج من الحدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال.
And as for if it is in the pattern of the third figure, and the disjunctive is real, its parts agreeing in quality and quantity, and the categoricals [equal] in number to the parts of the disjunction, and they agree in quantity, then the predicate in them must be one, since the subject in them is one; but if the predicate were one, the categoricals would not be multiple; rather the predicate in them must be multiple. Its condition for productivity is affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without [exclusive of] conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negatives. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity of the disjunctive, the universality and particularity of its parts, their affirmation and negation, and the affirmation and negation of the categoricals and their universality and particularity, are thirty-six conjunctions. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive, its parts universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative categoricals, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and every B is R.' Its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive [that is] exclusive of emptiness without [exclusive of] conjunction, with particular parts, namely: 'Always, either some B is C or some D is A and R,' i.e., no case is empty of the two; because if 'Every B is C' is true and 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R' [are true], then 'Some C is A and R'; and if 'Every B is D' is true and 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R', then 'Some D is A and R.' The second conjunction: the conjunction is as it was, except the categoricals are universal negatives, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and no B is A, and no B is R.' Its result: 'It does not avoid either that some C or some D is not A nor R.' The explanation is like the previous. The third conjunction: the conjunction is as it was, except the categoricals differ in quality, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and no B is R.' Its result: 'Either some C or some D is either A or not R.' And if the categoricals are particulars, then three other conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions may be formed with the disjunctive when its parts are universal affirmative, and the result in them is like the result in the first in each conjunction according to it. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then six other conjunctions parallel to these six conjunctions may be formed with the categoricals. And if its parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, then six other conjunctions may be formed with the categoricals, so the total becomes eighteen conjunctions. And from the disjunctive when it is particular affirmative, another eighteen conjunctions parallel to these eighteen conjunctions may be prepared, except that the result in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider them, for they are easy to consider with the verification of what we mentioned. And as for if the categoricals are fewer than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling in it is as previously in the case where they equal the number of parts of the disjunction, in the conditions and number of conjunctions, except that the result arises only from the predicates of the categoricals and what is excluded from the middle boundaries of the parts of the disjunction.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage defines the rules for productive compound syllogisms in the third figure when the major premise is a disjunctive (disjunctive) and the minor premises are categorical (all subject-predicate). The author first specifies conditions: the disjunctive must be real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction (mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam‘), its parts must agree in quality and quantity, and the number of categoricals must equal the parts of the disjunction. The categoricals must be universal if the disjunctive's parts are particular affirmative, or universal negatives. Then the total number of productive conjunctions is 36, considering all combinations of quantity and quality. The author illustrates the first three conjunctions with examples using variables (B, C, D, A, R). For instance, the first conjunction: universal affirmative disjunctive 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D' with universal affirmative categoricals 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R' yields a disjunctive conclusion 'Always, either some B is C or some D is A and R'. This is explained by the principle that if one part of the disjunction holds, the predicate from the categoricals applies to its subject. The second and third conjunctions vary the quality of the categoricals. The author then elaborates variations: when categoricals are particular, three more conjunctions arise; when disjunctive parts are particular negatives, six more; etc., ultimately reaching 18 and then 36 again when the disjunctive itself is particular. Finally, if categoricals are fewer than disjunctive parts, the same rules apply except the conclusion only includes predicates from the categoricals and excludes the middle terms from the disjunctive. This detailed enumeration serves as a comprehensive guide for constructing valid compound syllogisms in the third figure with disjunctive premises.

منفصلة
حقيقية

Key Takeaway
The productivity of a third figure compound syllogism with a disjunctive premise depends on the disjunctive being affirmative and real or exclusive, and the categoricals matching the quantity of the disjunctive parts.

First Three Conjunctions Explained
Author demonstrates first three productive conjunctions with variables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for any of the first three conjunctions of a third figure compound syllogism with a disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
The first three conjunctions show how different categorical qualities yield specific disjunctive conclusions.

The author provides concrete examples using variables (B, C, D, A, R) to illustrate the first three productive conjunctions. Each varies the quality of the categorical premises while keeping the disjunctive universal affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first conjunction
Examine the example: disjunctive universal affirmative (Always either every B is C or every B is D) with two universal affirmative categoricals (Every B is A, Every B is R). The conclusion is a disjunctive with particular parts (Always either some B is C or some D is A and R).
The conclusion follows because if every B is C and every B is A, then some C is A (via third figure); similarly for D.
2
Analyze the second conjunction
Same disjunctive but categoricals are universal negatives (No B is A, No B is R). Conclusion: either some C is not A nor R or some D is not A nor R.
The negation carries through.
3
Analyze the third conjunction
Categoricals differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. Conclusion becomes a disjunctive where the result is either A or not R, depending on which disjunct holds.
The mixed quality produces a mixed conclusion.
4
Generalize
Notice that the conclusion always has particular parts, even if the premises are universal. This is because the third figure yields particular conclusions.
This is a key property of the third figure.

الاقترانات والنتيجة فيها كالنتيجة في الأول في كل اقتران بحسبه. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات آخر ستة موازية لهذه الاقترانات الستة، وإن كانت أجزاؤها جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ستة فيكون المجموع ثمانية عشر اقتراناً. وقد يتهيأ من المنفصلة إذا كانت جزئية موجبة اقترانات أُخر ثمانية عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات الثمانية عشر، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها، فإنها سهلة الاعتبار، مع تحقيق ما ذكرنا وأما إن كانت الحَمْليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال فالحكم فيها على ما تقدم في ما إذا كانت بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة إنما تكون من محمولات الحملية، وما خرج من الحدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال.
And as for if it is in the pattern of the third figure, and the disjunctive is real, its parts agreeing in quality and quantity, and the categoricals [equal] in number to the parts of the disjunction, and they agree in quantity, then the predicate in them must be one, since the subject in them is one; but if the predicate were one, the categoricals would not be multiple; rather the predicate in them must be multiple. Its condition for productivity is affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without [exclusive of] conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negatives. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity of the disjunctive, the universality and particularity of its parts, their affirmation and negation, and the affirmation and negation of the categoricals and their universality and particularity, are thirty-six conjunctions. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive, its parts universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative categoricals, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and every B is R.' Its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive [that is] exclusive of emptiness without [exclusive of] conjunction, with particular parts, namely: 'Always, either some B is C or some D is A and R,' i.e., no case is empty of the two; because if 'Every B is C' is true and 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R' [are true], then 'Some C is A and R'; and if 'Every B is D' is true and 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R', then 'Some D is A and R.' The second conjunction: the conjunction is as it was, except the categoricals are universal negatives, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and no B is A, and no B is R.' Its result: 'It does not avoid either that some C or some D is not A nor R.' The explanation is like the previous. The third conjunction: the conjunction is as it was, except the categoricals differ in quality, as our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D, and every B is A, and no B is R.' Its result: 'Either some C or some D is either A or not R.' And if the categoricals are particulars, then three other conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions may be formed with the disjunctive when its parts are universal affirmative, and the result in them is like the result in the first in each conjunction according to it. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then six other conjunctions parallel to these six conjunctions may be formed with the categoricals. And if its parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, then six other conjunctions may be formed with the categoricals, so the total becomes eighteen conjunctions. And from the disjunctive when it is particular affirmative, another eighteen conjunctions parallel to these eighteen conjunctions may be prepared, except that the result in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider them, for they are easy to consider with the verification of what we mentioned. And as for if the categoricals are fewer than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling in it is as previously in the case where they equal the number of parts of the disjunction, in the conditions and number of conjunctions, except that the result arises only from the predicates of the categoricals and what is excluded from the middle boundaries of the parts of the disjunction.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage defines the rules for productive compound syllogisms in the third figure when the major premise is a disjunctive (disjunctive) and the minor premises are categorical (all subject-predicate). The author first specifies conditions: the disjunctive must be real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction (mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam‘), its parts must agree in quality and quantity, and the number of categoricals must equal the parts of the disjunction. The categoricals must be universal if the disjunctive's parts are particular affirmative, or universal negatives. Then the total number of productive conjunctions is 36, considering all combinations of quantity and quality. The author illustrates the first three conjunctions with examples using variables (B, C, D, A, R). For instance, the first conjunction: universal affirmative disjunctive 'Always, either every B is C or every B is D' with universal affirmative categoricals 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is R' yields a disjunctive conclusion 'Always, either some B is C or some D is A and R'. This is explained by the principle that if one part of the disjunction holds, the predicate from the categoricals applies to its subject. The second and third conjunctions vary the quality of the categoricals. The author then elaborates variations: when categoricals are particular, three more conjunctions arise; when disjunctive parts are particular negatives, six more; etc., ultimately reaching 18 and then 36 again when the disjunctive itself is particular. Finally, if categoricals are fewer than disjunctive parts, the same rules apply except the conclusion only includes predicates from the categoricals and excludes the middle terms from the disjunctive. This detailed enumeration serves as a comprehensive guide for constructing valid compound syllogisms in the third figure with disjunctive premises.

نتيجة
موجبة
سالبة

Key Takeaway
The first three conjunctions demonstrate how the quality of categorical premises affects the conclusion, which is always a disjunctive with particular parts.

Enumerating the 36 Conjunctions
Author enumerates how 36 conjunctions arise from variations in quantity and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 36 productive conjunctions for third figure compound syllogisms by varying quantity and quality of premises.

Core Idea
The 36 conjunctions arise from systematic variation of the disjunctive's quantity, its parts' quantity and quality, and the categoricals' quantity and quality.

After illustrating the basic conjunctions, the author expands the enumeration by considering variations: when categoricals are particular, when disjunctive parts are particular negatives, and when the disjunctive itself is particular. Each variation multiplies the number of productive conjunctions, reaching a total of 36.

Guided Path
1
Track the base set
Start with disjunctive universal affirmative, its parts universal affirmative. This yields three conjunctions (from Lesson 2). Then, if categoricals are particular, three more parallel conjunctions arise (total 6).
The passage says: 'وإن كانت الحَمْليات جزئيات فقد يأتلف ... ثلاثة أخر'
2
Vary the disjunctive parts
If the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, six more conjunctions (parallel to the previous six) arise. If the parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, another six, making 18.
The passage counts: 6 + 6 + 6 = 18.
3
Consider the disjunctive as particular
If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, another 18 conjunctions parallel to the previous 18 arise, making 36 total.
The conclusion in this case becomes particular affirmative disjunctive.
4
Handle fewer categoricals
If categoricals are fewer than disjunctive parts, the same conditions apply, but the conclusion only involves the predicates of the categoricals and excludes the unpaired disjuncts.
This is a separate clause.

Classifying Conjunctive Syllogisms with Disjunctive and Categorical Premises
Enumeration of conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the possible conjunctive syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise with categorical premises, given the quantity and quality of the disjuncts and categoricals.

Core Idea
The number of valid conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctives varies systematically with the quantity and quality of the propositions.

In this passage, the author systematically counts all possible valid conjunctive syllogisms (iqtiranat) that combine a disjunctive proposition (munfasila) with categorical propositions (hamliya). This enumeration is not arbitrary; it depends on whether the disjuncts are universal/particular, affirmative/negative, and on the type of disjunction. Understanding this classification helps you see the scope and limits of such syllogisms in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base cases
The author starts with six conjunctives (not detailed in this chunk, but referenced). Recognize that these are the starting point for further variations.
The 'six conjunctives' likely refer to previous material on basic forms without disjunctives.
2
Add variations in disjunct quality
Note that if disjuncts are particular negatives, six more arise; if particular affirmatives or universal negatives, another six, totalling eighteen.
The author says 'another six' each, so total base 6 + 6 + 6 = 18.
3
Include the case of a particular affirmative disjunctive
From a particular affirmative disjunctive, another eighteen parallel to the previous eighteen can be formed, but with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion.
This makes 36 total from universal disjunctives? Actually the text says 'eighteen conjunctives' from a particular affirmative disjunctive, not cumulative.
4
Understand the role of quantity of categoricals
When categoricals are fewer than disjuncts, the conditions and number of conjunctives remain the same, but the conclusion changes to include the subjects of the categoricals and the disjuncts outside the middle terms.
The author asserts the ruling is 'as previously stated' for conditions and count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول في كل اقتران بحسبه. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات آخر ستة موازية لهذه الاقترانات الستة، وإن كانت أجزاؤها جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ستة فيكون المجموع ثمانية عشر اقتراناً. وقد يتهيأ من المنفصلة إذا كانت جزئية موجبة اقترانات أُخر ثمانية عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات الثمانية عشر، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها، فإنها سهلة الاعتبار، مع تحقيق ما ذكرنا وأما إن كانت الحَمْليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال فالحكم فيها على ما تقدم في ما إذا كانت بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة إنما تكون من محمولات الحملية، وما خرج من الحدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال.
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الرابع والحَمْليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال والمحمول في الكل واحد، ولكن أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية والحَمْليات متحدة الكمية فشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات إن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة، وإن كانت الحَمْليات جزئيات موجبة فإن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقترانا:
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّات موجبة وكليات موجبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "أ" "ب") و(كل "ر" "ب") ونتيجته منفصلة موجبة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" "أ" و "ر").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليّات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" ولا شيء من "أ" "ب") و (لا شيء من "ر" "ب") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ" ولا "ر").
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "أ" "ب") و(لا شيء من "ر" "ب") ونتيجته دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" "" وليس بعض "ر"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وبعض "أ" "ب") و(بعض "د" "ب") ونتيجته كالاقتران الأول.
وأما إن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب فقد تأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات الكلية اقترانان آخران. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات الكلية أيضاً اقترانان آخران.
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة فقد تأتلف منها مع الحمليات أربعة اقترانات أخرى موازية للأربعة الأولى، فيكون المجموع اثني عشر [اقترانا]. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة من الكلية مع الحَمْليات، إلا أن النتيجة تكون فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
وأما إن كان المحمول في الحَمْليات متعدداً فهي غير متصور؛ ضرورة أن الغرض فيها إذا كان الموضع الأجزاء المنفصلة واحداً، وهو المحمول في الحمليات في هذا الشكل.
وأما إن كانت الحمليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال فالحكم في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، فكما لو كانت الحَمْليات مساوية لأجزاء الانفصال، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون من موضوع الحَمْليات والخارج عن حدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال فاعتبرها بنفسك.
وأما إن كان الموضوع لأجزاء الانفصال متعدداً فالمحمول فيها إما متحد أو متعدد، فإن كان متحداً فالحَمْلية إما متحدة أو متكثرة، فإن كانت متحدة فإن كان الحد الأوسط على نسق الشكل الأول، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال
First, regarding each conjunctive [syllogism] according to its [type]. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then there may be composed from them with categoricals another six conjunctives parallel to these six conjunctives. And if its parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, then there may be composed from them with categoricals another six conjunctives, so the total becomes eighteen conjunctives. And from the disjunctive, if it is a particular affirmative, there may be prepared another eighteen conjunctives parallel to these eighteen conjunctives, except that the conclusion therein is a particular affirmative disjunctive. So consider it, for it is easy to consider, with verification of what we mentioned. And as for if the categoricals are less than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling therein is as previously stated when they are equal to the number of the parts of the disjunction, regarding conditions and number of conjunctives, except that the conclusion is only from the predicates of the categoricals and what comes out of the middle terms from the parts of the disjunction. And as for if it is on the pattern of the fourth figure, and the categoricals are equal to the number of the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in all is one, but the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and the categoricals are uniform in quantity, then the condition for its production is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives or particular affirmatives; and if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Its productive conjunctives are twenty-four: The first conjunctive: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal affirmative categoricals, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and every A is B, and every R is B', and its conclusion is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction, which is 'always either some C or some D is A and R'. The second conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and no A is B, and no R is B', and its conclusion is 'always it is not the case that either some C or some D is not A and not R'. The third conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are of different quality, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and every A is B, and no R is B', and its conclusion is 'always it is not the case that either some C or some D is and some R is not'. The fourth conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are particular affirmatives, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and some A is B, and some D is B', and its conclusion is like the first conjunctive. And as for if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, then there may be composed from them with the universal categoricals another two conjunctives. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, then there may be composed from them with the universal categoricals also another two conjunctives. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then there may be composed from them with the categoricals another four conjunctives parallel to the first four, so the total becomes twelve [conjunctives]. And if the disjunctive is a particular affirmative, there may be composed from it with the categoricals another twelve conjunctives parallel to the conjunctives arising from the universal with the categoricals, except that the conclusion therein is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider it yourself. And as for if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, it is inconceivable; necessarily because the purpose therein, when the place of the parts of the disjunctive is one, which is the predicate in the categoricals in this figure. And as for if the categoricals are less than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling regarding conditions and number of conjunctives is as if the categoricals were equal to the parts of the disjunction, except that the conclusion therein is from the subject of the categoricals and what is outside the middle limits from the parts of the disjunction. So consider it yourself. And as for if the subject of the parts of the disjunction is multiple, then the predicate therein is either one or multiple. If it is one, then the categorical is either one or multiple. If it is one, then if the middle term is on the pattern of the first figure, and let us posit the speech when the parts of the disjunction...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification of valid conjunctive syllogisms (iqtiranat) that combine disjunctive (munfasila) and categorical (hamliya) propositions. The author begins by noting that when the parts (disjuncts) of the disjunctive proposition are particular negatives, six new conjunctives parallel to previous ones can be formed. Similarly, if the parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, another six, totalling eighteen. From a particular affirmative disjunctive, another eighteen parallel to these can be made, but with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. Next, rules are given for cases where categoricals are fewer than disjuncts: conditions and number of conjunctives remain the same, but the conclusion uses the predicates of the categoricals and the disjuncts outside the middle terms.

The author then focuses on the pattern of the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi'), where categoricals equal the number of disjuncts, the predicate is the same in all, and the disjuncts and categoricals are uniform in quantity and quality. Conditions for validity: the disjunctive must be affirmative (either real disjunction or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction), and the categoricals must be universal if the disjuncts are universal negatives or particular affirmatives; if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, the disjuncts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Under these conditions, twenty-four productive conjunctives arise. The author then details the first four of these twenty-four, giving examples with variables (A, B, C, D, R) and showing the disjunctive conclusions (e.g., 'always either some C or some D is A and R' for the first).

After listing these four, the author says that if the disjuncts are universal negatives, two further conjunctives with universal categoricals arise; if particular affirmatives, another two; if particular negatives, four more (parallel to the first four), totalling twelve. From a particular affirmative disjunctive, another twelve parallel to those from the universal disjunctive can be formed, but with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. The author then notes that if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, such combinations are inconceivable because the purpose requires a single predicate. If the categoricals are fewer than disjuncts, the ruling is as before. Finally, the author begins discussing cases where the subject of the disjuncts is multiple, but the passage is cut off.

Throughout, the passage functions as a systematic expansion of possible syllogistic forms involving disjunctive propositions, providing conditions for validity and counting the resulting valid moods.

Replace B, C, D, A, R with Islamic terms (e.g., B=mukallaf, C=obligated to pray, D=obligated to fast, A=Muslim, R=adult) and state the conclusion. The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Conditions for Validity in Fourth-Figure Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms
Conditions for valid fourth-figure disjunctive-categorical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given fourth-figure conjunctive syllogism with a disjunctive proposition and categoricals satisfies the conditions for a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Validity in the fourth figure requires the disjunctive to be affirmative and of a specific type, with matching quantities between disjuncts and categoricals.

The passage provides a detailed set of conditions for the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi') when categoricals equal the number of disjuncts, the predicate is identical, and both are uniform in quantity and quality. These conditions determine the twenty-four productive conjunctives listed. Understanding these conditions allows you to sift valid from invalid forms.

Guided Path
1
Check the disjunctive type
The disjunctive must be affirmative and either real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'). Exclusive disjunctions (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw) are not valid here.
The passage explicitly says 'إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع'.
2
Check the quantity of categoricals relative to disjuncts
If disjuncts are universal negatives or particular affirmatives, the categoricals must be universal. If categoricals are particular affirmatives, the disjuncts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives.
This is a condition of matching quantities.
3
Verify uniformity
All disjuncts must be uniform in quantity and quality (e.g., all universal affirmatives), and all categoricals must be uniform in quantity (e.g., all universal or all particular).
The passage says 'أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية والحَمْليات متحدة الكمية'.
4
Identify the conclusion form
The conclusion is a disjunctive proposition that is affirmative and of the same type (exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction). It always uses the subjects of the categoricals and the predicates of the disjuncts in a particular form.
For example, 'دائماً إما بعض ج وإما بعض د أ ر'

الأول في كل اقتران بحسبه. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات آخر ستة موازية لهذه الاقترانات الستة، وإن كانت أجزاؤها جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ستة فيكون المجموع ثمانية عشر اقتراناً. وقد يتهيأ من المنفصلة إذا كانت جزئية موجبة اقترانات أُخر ثمانية عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات الثمانية عشر، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها، فإنها سهلة الاعتبار، مع تحقيق ما ذكرنا وأما إن كانت الحَمْليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال فالحكم فيها على ما تقدم في ما إذا كانت بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة إنما تكون من محمولات الحملية، وما خرج من الحدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال.

الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليّات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" ولا شيء من "أ" "ب") و (لا شيء من "ر" "ب") ونتيجته (دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ" ولا "ر").

وأما إن كان الموضوع لأجزاء الانفصال متعدداً فالمحمول فيها إما متحد أو متعدد، فإن كان متحداً فالحَمْلية إما متحدة أو متكثرة، فإن كانت متحدة فإن كان الحد الأوسط على نسق الشكل الأول، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال
First, regarding each conjunctive [syllogism] according to its [type]. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then there may be composed from them with categoricals another six conjunctives parallel to these six conjunctives. And if its parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, then there may be composed from them with categoricals another six conjunctives, so the total becomes eighteen conjunctives. And from the disjunctive, if it is a particular affirmative, there may be prepared another eighteen conjunctives parallel to these eighteen conjunctives, except that the conclusion therein is a particular affirmative disjunctive. So consider it, for it is easy to consider, with verification of what we mentioned. And as for if the categoricals are less than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling therein is as previously stated when they are equal to the number of the parts of the disjunction, regarding conditions and number of conjunctives, except that the conclusion is only from the predicates of the categoricals and what comes out of the middle terms from the parts of the disjunction. And as for if it is on the pattern of the fourth figure, and the categoricals are equal to the number of the parts of the disjunction, and the predicate in all is one, but the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and the categoricals are uniform in quantity, then the condition for its production is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives or particular affirmatives; and if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Its productive conjunctives are twenty-four: The first conjunctive: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal affirmative categoricals, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and every A is B, and every R is B', and its conclusion is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction, which is 'always either some C or some D is A and R'. The second conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and no A is B, and no R is B', and its conclusion is 'always it is not the case that either some C or some D is not A and not R'. The third conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are of different quality, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and every A is B, and no R is B', and its conclusion is 'always it is not the case that either some C or some D is and some R is not'. The fourth conjunctive: the conjunctive is the same, except that the categoricals are particular affirmatives, as our saying: 'always either every B is C or every B is D, and some A is B, and some D is B', and its conclusion is like the first conjunctive. And as for if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, then there may be composed from them with the universal categoricals another two conjunctives. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, then there may be composed from them with the universal categoricals also another two conjunctives. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then there may be composed from them with the categoricals another four conjunctives parallel to the first four, so the total becomes twelve [conjunctives]. And if the disjunctive is a particular affirmative, there may be composed from it with the categoricals another twelve conjunctives parallel to the conjunctives arising from the universal with the categoricals, except that the conclusion therein is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider it yourself. And as for if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, it is inconceivable; necessarily because the purpose therein, when the place of the parts of the disjunctive is one, which is the predicate in the categoricals in this figure. And as for if the categoricals are less than the parts of the disjunction, the ruling regarding conditions and number of conjunctives is as if the categoricals were equal to the parts of the disjunction, except that the conclusion therein is from the subject of the categoricals and what is outside the middle limits from the parts of the disjunction. So consider it yourself. And as for if the subject of the parts of the disjunction is multiple, then the predicate therein is either one or multiple. If it is one, then the categorical is either one or multiple. If it is one, then if the middle term is on the pattern of the first figure, and let us posit the speech when the parts of the disjunction...

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification of valid conjunctive syllogisms (iqtiranat) that combine disjunctive (munfasila) and categorical (hamliya) propositions. The author begins by noting that when the parts (disjuncts) of the disjunctive proposition are particular negatives, six new conjunctives parallel to previous ones can be formed. Similarly, if the parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, another six, totalling eighteen. From a particular affirmative disjunctive, another eighteen parallel to these can be made, but with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. Next, rules are given for cases where categoricals are fewer than disjuncts: conditions and number of conjunctives remain the same, but the conclusion uses the predicates of the categoricals and the disjuncts outside the middle terms.

The author then focuses on the pattern of the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi'), where categoricals equal the number of disjuncts, the predicate is the same in all, and the disjuncts and categoricals are uniform in quantity and quality. Conditions for validity: the disjunctive must be affirmative (either real disjunction or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction), and the categoricals must be universal if the disjuncts are universal negatives or particular affirmatives; if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, the disjuncts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Under these conditions, twenty-four productive conjunctives arise. The author then details the first four of these twenty-four, giving examples with variables (A, B, C, D, R) and showing the disjunctive conclusions (e.g., 'always either some C or some D is A and R' for the first).

After listing these four, the author says that if the disjuncts are universal negatives, two further conjunctives with universal categoricals arise; if particular affirmatives, another two; if particular negatives, four more (parallel to the first four), totalling twelve. From a particular affirmative disjunctive, another twelve parallel to those from the universal disjunctive can be formed, but with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. The author then notes that if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, such combinations are inconceivable because the purpose requires a single predicate. If the categoricals are fewer than disjuncts, the ruling is as before. Finally, the author begins discussing cases where the subject of the disjuncts is multiple, but the passage is cut off.

Throughout, the passage functions as a systematic expansion of possible syllogistic forms involving disjunctive propositions, providing conditions for validity and counting the resulting valid moods.

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, the disjunctive must be affirmative and of a specific type, and the quantities must match in a particular way to produce a valid conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the pattern of the first conjunctive given in the passage: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D; every A is B; every R is B.' Replace B, C, D, A, R with Islamic theological terms (e.g., B=act of God, C=eternal, D=temporally originated, A=creation, R=miracle). The Third and Fourth Iqtiranat: Mixed Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms
Author enumerates the twelve iqtiranats with examples of the third and fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the third and fourth iqtiranat from a disjunctive and categorical premise, and identify the correct conclusion.

Core Idea
The third and fourth iqtiranat combine a disjunctive with differently qualified categoricals, producing disjunctive conclusions.

In this lesson, we examine two specific compound syllogisms from the set of twelve: the third and fourth iqtiranat. The author provides concrete examples with logical variables. By mastering these two, you gain the pattern for the remaining forms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the disjunctive premise
In the third iqtiran, the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is J or every B is D'. Identify its two parts: 'every B is J' and 'every B is D'.
The disjunctive is assumed to be exclusive or inclusive? The passage does not specify, but the form works for both.
2
Identify the categorical premises
There are two categorical premises: 'every A is B' (universal affirmative) and 'no R is B' (universal negative). Note their different qualities.
These are the hamliyyat mentioned; they share the middle term B with the disjunctive.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is a disjunctive: 'either some J or some D' (from the first categorical) and 'not some R' (from the second). The middle term B is eliminated.
The conclusion has two parts: a disjunctive about J and D, and a negative about R.
4
Compare with the fourth iqtiran
The fourth iqtiran is identical except the categoricals are particular affirmatives: 'some A is B' and 'some D is B'. Its conclusion is similar in form.
The change in categorical quantity affects the conclusion's quantity but maintains the disjunctive structure.
5
Note the systematic variation
These two examples are the base cases. By changing the quality and quantity of the disjunctive's parts and the categoricals, the author derives the full set of twelve.
Understanding these two helps you generate the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وكل "أ" "ب") و(لا شيء من "ر" "ب") ونتيجته دائماً لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض "ج" وإما بعض "د" "" وليس بعض "ر"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د" وبعض "أ" "ب") و(بعض "د" "ب") ونتيجته كالاقتران الأول.
وأما إن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب فقد تأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات الكلية اقترانان آخران. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات الكلية أيضاً اقترانان آخران.
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة فقد تأتلف منها مع الحمليات أربعة اقترانات أخرى موازية للأربعة الأولى، فيكون المجموع اثني عشر [اقترانا]. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة من الكلية مع الحَمْليات، إلا أن النتيجة تكون فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
Continuation of the first part on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the categoricals are of different quality, such as our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D, and every A is B' and 'No R is B', and its conclusion is always either some J or some D '' and not some R. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the categoricals are particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D, and some A is B' and 'some D is B', and its conclusion is like the first conjunction. As for if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, then two other conjunctions may be composed from them with universal categoricals. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, then also two other conjunctions may be composed from them with universal categoricals. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then four other conjunctions may be composed from them parallel to the first four, so the total is twelve conjunctions. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then twelve other conjunctions may be composed from it with the categoricals parallel to the conjunctions occurring from the universal with the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the compound syllogisms (iqtiranat) that combine a disjunctive premise (al-munfasila) with a categorical premise (al-hamliyya). The author first lists the third and fourth conjunctions, which are variants based on the quality and quantity of the categorical premises. Then he generalizes: when the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, two new conjunctions arise; when they are particular affirmatives, another two; when they are particular negatives, four more, totaling twelve. Finally, if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, another set of twelve parallels the previous ones but yields a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. The author leaves the reader to derive the specific forms. The use of variables (ب, ج, د, etc.) follows standard logical notation.

Checkpoint
In the third iqtiran, if the categoricals are 'every A is B' and 'no R is B', what is the form of the conclusion?
A) A disjunctive: either some J or some D, and a separate statement that not some R
B) A categorical: all J is D
C) A disjunctive: either all J or all D
D) A conditional: if some J then some D

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth iqtiranat demonstrate how a disjunctive and categorical premises combine to yield a disjunctive conclusion, with the middle term removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the pattern of the third iqtiran, construct an iqtiran with the disjunctive 'Either every fasting person is rewarded or every fasting person is not rewarded' and a universal categorical 'No Muslim is unrewarded', and state the conclusion.
?
Determine whether the following is a valid iqtiran: 'Always either every prayer is valid or every prayer is invalid; some action is prayer; therefore, either some action is valid or some action is invalid.'

General Patterns: Enumerating the Twelve Iqtiranat
Author generalizes the enumeration of twelve iqtiranats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the twelve iqtiranat formed by varying the quality and quantity of the disjunctive's parts and the categorical premise.

Core Idea
Twelve distinct iqtiranat arise from systematic variation of disjunctive part quality and categorical quantity.

Building on the specific examples, the author now generalizes. By changing the quality (universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) of the disjunctive's parts and pairing them with universal or particular categoricals, he derives twelve valid compound syllogisms. This systematic approach exemplifies the power of logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Review the base cases
The third and fourth iqtiranat used universal affirmative parts. Consider when the parts are universal negatives: e.g., 'always either no B is J or no B is D'.
This changes the inference pattern.
2
Apply the pattern for universal negative parts
With universal categoricals, two new iqtiranat emerge. The author states: 'if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, then two other conjunctions may be composed from them with universal categoricals.'
The precise forms are left to the reader, but mirror the third and fourth mutatis mutandis.
3
Consider particular affirmative parts
If the disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives (e.g., 'always either some B is J or some B is D'), then with universal categoricals, two more iqtiranat arise.
Again, the pattern is parallel to the base cases.
4
Consider particular negative parts
If the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, then four iqtiranat are formed, corresponding to the first four but with negative parts.
The number doubles because each of the two base cases now has a variant with particular negative disjunctive parts.
5
Special case: particular affirmative disjunctive
If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative (e.g., 'sometimes either every B is J or every B is D'), then another set of twelve iqtiranat results, but with particular affirmative disjunctive conclusions.
The author leaves this to the reader: 'so consider it yourself.'

Compound Syllogisms with Uniform Disjuncts in the First Figure
Conditions for compound syllogisms with uniform disjuncts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid compound syllogism with a uniform disjunctive premise and a categorical premise in the first figure, and construct one using Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Sixteen productive conjunctions yield certain conclusions only under three precise conditions.

Compound syllogisms combine a disjunctive premise with a categorical one. In this passage, the author specifies the conditions under which such a syllogism is productive when the disjunctive parts are uniform in quality and quantity and the categorical follows the first figure. He then enumerates the sixteen possible conjunctions, each yielding an exclusive disjunctive conclusion. Understanding these conditions is crucial for recognizing valid compound arguments in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for productivity
The author states: the disjunctive must be affirmative, it must be man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam' (inclusive disjunction), and the categorical must be universal. These are necessary for the conclusion to follow.
2
Recognize the pattern of the first figure
The middle term (hadd awsat) must be in the subject of the categorical and predicate of the disjunctive parts, matching the first-figure arrangement where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other.
3
List the sixteen conjunctions conceptually
There are four possibilities for the disjunctive (affirmative/negative, categorical) and four for the categorical (affirmative/negative, and the quality of the disjunctive parts), making sixteen combinations. All produce an exclusive disjunctive conclusion.
4
Apply the first conjunction to an Islamic example
Replace variables with real terms. For example, 'Always either every legal capacity (B) is a condition of validity (J) or every puberty (D) is a condition of validity (J)' and 'Every condition of validity (J) is required for the contract (A)' yields 'Always either every legal capacity is required for the contract or every puberty is required for the contract.'
