وأما إن كان المحمول في الحَمْليات متعدداً فهي غير متصور؛ ضرورة أن الغرض فيها إذا كان الموضع الأجزاء المنفصلة واحداً، وهو المحمول في الحمليات في هذا الشكل.
وأما إن كانت الحمليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال فالحكم في الشروط، وعدد الاقترانات، فكما لو كانت الحَمْليات مساوية لأجزاء الانفصال، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون من موضوع الحَمْليات والخارج عن حدود الوسطى من أجزاء الانفصال فاعتبرها بنفسك.
وأما إن كان الموضوع لأجزاء الانفصال متعدداً فالمحمول فيها إما متحد أو متعدد، فإن كان متحداً فالحَمْلية إما متحدة أو متكثرة، فإن كانت متحدة فإن كان الحد الأوسط على نسق الشكل الأول، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال متحدة الكيفية والكمية - وأنت تعرفه في المختلطات - فشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحملية كلية، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل مانعة الخلو دون الجمع: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإمـا كـل "د" "ج") و(كـل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته كليـة موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، موضوعات أجزاء الانفصال فيها موضوعات المنفصلة، ومحمولها محمول الحَمْلية، وهي دائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "د" "أ") وإنما قلنا بكونها مانعة الخلو دون الجمع لجواز أن يكون "أ" ثابتاً للجزأين. الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية سالبة، ونتيجته (لا يخلو إما أن يكون لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ").
الاقتران الثالث من كلية موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها جزئيات سالبة، وكليّة حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "د" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته كالاقتران الأول، وهي دائماً إمـا كـل "ب" "أ" وإمـا كـل "د" "أ") لأنه إن صدق (ليس كل "ب" "أ") صدق كل "د" "أ") وإن صدق (ليس كل "د" "أ") صدق (كل "ب" "ج").
الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية سالبة، ونتيجته كالاقتران الثاني. الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها جزئيات موجبة وحَمْلية كلية موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع أجزاؤها جزئية موجبة، وهي (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "أ" وإما بعض "د" "ب").

الاقتران السابع من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّات سوالب وحملية كلية موجبة، ونتيجته كالاقتران الخامس؛ لأنه إن صدق لا شيء من "ب" "ج") صدق
As for if the predicate in the categoricals is multiple, then it is inconceivable; of necessity the purpose in them when the place of the parts of the disjunctive is one, and it is the predicate in the categoricals in this figure. As for if the categoricals are fewer than the parts of the disjunction, then the judgment regarding the conditions and number of conjunctions is as if the categoricals are equal to the parts of the disjunction, except that the conclusion in them is from the subject of the categoricals and what falls outside the bounds of the middle from the parts of the disjunction, so consider it yourself. As for if the subject of the parts of the disjunction is multiple, then the predicate in them is either one or multiple. If it is one, then the categorical is either one or multiple. If it is one, and the middle term is in the pattern of the first figure, and let us suppose the discourse when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity - and you know that from the mixed syllogisms - then the condition for its production is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be genuine exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), and that the categorical be universal. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen, and the conclusion in all is exclusive disjunction. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, such as our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Every J is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction exclusive, the subjects of its parts being the subjects of the disjunction, and its predicate the predicate of the categorical, namely 'Always either every B is A or every D is A'. And we only said it is exclusive because it is possible that A is affirmed of both parts. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is 'it is not empty of either no B is A or no D is A'. The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are particular negatives and a universal affirmative categorical, such as our saying: 'Always either not every B is J or not every D is J' and 'Every J is A', and its conclusion is like the first conjunction, namely 'Always either every B is A or every D is A', because if 'not every B is A' is true, then 'every D is A' is true, and if 'not every D is A' is true, then 'every B is J' is true. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is like the second conjunction. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are particular affirmatives and a universal affirmative categorical, such as our saying: 'Always either some B is J or some D is J' and 'Every J is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction whose parts are particular affirmative, namely 'Always either some B is A or some D is B'. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is either always some B is not A or some D is not ( ). The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal negatives and a universal affirmative categorical, and its conclusion is like the fifth conjunction; because if 'no B is J' is true, then...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions and enumeration of compound syllogisms formed from a disjunctive premise and a categorical premise when the disjunctive parts have a common subject. The author first dismisses the case where the categorical has multiple predicates as impossible. Then he addresses cases where the categoricals are fewer than the disjunctive parts, stating that the rules are similar to the case of equality, with the conclusion derived from the subject of the categoricals and the remaining disjunctive parts. The main focus is when the subject of the disjunctive parts is multiple and the predicate is one. Here, if the categorical is one and the middle term follows the first figure, and the disjunctive parts are uniform in quality and quantity, the conditions for productivity are: the disjunctive must be affirmative, genuine exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), and the categorical must be universal. Under these conditions, there are sixteen productive conjunctions, all yielding an exclusive disjunctive conclusion. The author then lists the first seven conjunctions with examples, explaining how the conclusion is reached. For instance, the first conjunction combines a universal affirmative disjunction (every B is J or every D is J) with a universal affirmative categorical (every J is A) to yield a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction (every B is A or every D is A). The disjunction is exclusive because A could be true of both B and D. The third conjunction uses a disjunctive with particular negative parts (not every B is J or not every D is J) and a universal affirmative categorical, leading to a universal affirmative conclusion through a mutual implication. The text ends mid-sentence during the seventh conjunction.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
حد أوسط

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism with a uniform disjunctive and a categorical premise is productive only if the disjunctive is affirmative and inclusive, and the categorical is universal, yielding sixteen possible conjunctions.

First Figure Disjunctive Syllogisms: Eight Productive Conjunctions
Enumeration of eight productive conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the eight productive conjunctions for a first figure mixed syllogism with a universal positive disjunctive premise and derive the correct conclusion.

Core Idea
The conclusion mirrors the disjunction's parts with the categorical's predicate.

This passage systematically enumerates eight valid combinations (conjunctions) for a first figure mixed syllogism where one premise is a universal positive disjunctive and the other is a universal categorical. The author specifies conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and exclusive (man'at al-khuluw without al-jam'), and the categorical must be universal. Understanding these conjunctions is essential for constructing and evaluating arguments that use alternative premises, a common tool in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the productivity conditions
Note the three conditions: the disjunctive premise must be affirmative (positive), true, and of the type 'man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'' (exclusive: at least one part is true). The categorical premise must be universal (either positive or negative). Check these before proceeding.
The disjunctive may have parts of varying quantity and quality.
2
Analyze the first two conjunctions
In the first conjunction, both parts of the disjunctive are universal positives, and the categorical is universal positive. The conclusion is a universal positive disjunction with the same subjects and the categorical's predicate. In the second, the categorical is universal negative, yielding a universal negative disjunction. Observe the pattern.
Example: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Every J is A' gives 'Always either every B is A or every D is A'.
3
Analyze conjunctions 3 and 4
Here the disjunctive parts are particular negatives (e.g., 'not every B is J'). With a universal positive categorical, the conclusion is a universal positive disjunction (like the first). With a universal negative categorical, the conclusion is a universal negative disjunction (like the second). Understand the reasoning: the exclusive disjunction forces the truth of the other part when one is false.
Proof: if 'not every B is J' is true, then 'every D is J' is true (from exclusive disjunction), and since all J are A, 'every D is A' follows.
4
Analyze conjunctions 5 and 6
The disjunctive parts are particular positives (e.g., 'some B is J'). With a universal positive categorical, the conclusion is a universal positive disjunction with particular positive parts. With a universal negative categorical, the conclusion is a universal disjunction with particular negative parts. Note the variation in quantity.
Example: 'Always either some B is J or some D is J' and 'Every J is A' gives 'Always either some B is A or some D is A'.
5
Analyze conjunctions 7 and 8
The disjunctive parts are universal negatives (e.g., 'no B is J'). The conclusions are the same as in conjunctions 5 and 6, respectively. The proof relies on the exclusive disjunction: if 'no B is J' is true, then 'some D is J' is true (since at least one part must be true), and so on. Observe that the quantity changes from universal to particular due to the nature of the parts.
This shows the interplay between the quantity of the disjunctive parts and the categorical.

Continuation of the fourth article on defining the forms of arguments unified in quality and quantity — and you know it in the mixed ones — so the condition for its productivity is positivity of the disjunctive, and that it be true, exclusive (preventing emptiness without combination), and that the categorical be universal. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is exclusive (preventing emptiness without combination). The first conjunction: from two universal positives and the parts of the disjunction are universal positives, as we say: (Always either every B is J or every D is J) and (Every J is A). Its conclusion is a universal positive disjunctive exclusive (preventing emptiness without combination), the subjects of the parts of the disjunction are the subjects of the disjunctive, and their predicate is the predicate of the categorical, and it is always either every B is A or every D is A. We only say it is exclusive due to the possibility that A is affirmed for both parts. The second conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is (It does not lack that either no B is A or no D is A). The third conjunction: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are particular negatives, and a universal positive categorical, as we say: (Always either not every B is J or not every D is J) and (Every J is A). Its conclusion is like the first conjunction, and it is always either every B is A or every D is A, because if 'not every B is A' is true, then 'every D is A' is true? [text has 'ليس كل ب أ' and 'كل د أ'] Actually the proof: because if 'not every B is J' is true, then 'every D is J' is true, and 'Every J is A', so 'every D is A'; and if 'not every D is J' is true, then 'every B is J' is true, and 'Every J is A', so 'every B is A'. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is like the second. The fifth conjunction: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are particular positives, and a universal positive categorical, as we say: (Always either some B is J or some D is J) and (Every J is A). Its conclusion is a universal positive disjunctive exclusive whose parts are particular positive, and it is (Always either some B is A or some D is A). The sixth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is (Always either some B is not A or some D is not A). The seventh conjunction: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are universal negatives, and a universal positive categorical, and its conclusion is like the fifth conjunction, because if 'no B is J' is true, then 'some D is J' is true, and if 'no D is J' is true, then 'some B is J' is true. The eighth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except the categorical in it is universal negative, and its conclusion is like the sixth conjunction, due to the explanation we mentioned.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first eight productive conjunctions for the first figure when the disjunctive premise is universal positive and the categorical premise is universal. The author begins by stating the conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative (positive), must be a true exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw without al-jam'), and the categorical premise must be universal. There are sixteen such conjunctions in total, but here the focus is on the case where the disjunctive is universal positive. The conclusion in all cases is a disjunction that is exclusive (man'at al-khuluw without al-jam'). The first conjunction (iqtiran) has both premises universal positive: e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Every J is A'. The conclusion is 'Always either every B is A or every D is A'. The author notes that the conclusion is exclusive because A could belong to both B and D. The second conjunction is similar but the categorical is universal negative, giving a conclusion that is a universal negative disjunction ('It does not lack that either no B is A or no D is A'). The third conjunction has the disjunctive with particular negative parts and a universal positive categorical, yielding the same conclusion as the first. The explanation uses the truth conditions: if 'not every B is J' is true, then 'every D is J' must be true (given the exclusive disjunction), and since all J are A, we get 'every D is A', etc. The fourth is like the second but with the same disjunctive parts. The fifth conjunction has the disjunctive with particular positive parts, giving a conclusion with particular positive parts. The sixth is like the fifth but with a negative categorical. The seventh has the disjunctive with universal negative parts (e.g., 'no B is J or no D is J'), and the conclusion is like the fifth. The eighth is like the seventh but with a negative categorical. This enumeration is systematic, showing how varying the quality and quantity of the parts of the disjunction and the categorical premise yields different conclusions. The commentary notes that the condition 'man'at al-khuluw without al-jam'' ensures that the two parts cannot both be false (they cannot be empty together) but they can both be true (they are not exclusive in the sense of preventing combination). This is a key distinction.

Key Takeaway
The eight conjunctions show that a valid mixed disjunctive syllogism in the first figure always yields a disjunctive conclusion that mirrors the parts of the disjunction with the categorical's predicate, while preserving the exclusive condition.

Deriving the Eight Conjunctions for a Particular Positive Disjunctive
Author states that eight parallel conjunctions exist for particular positive disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the eight productive conjunctions for a first-figure syllogism with a particular positive disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
Eight parallel conjunctions exist when the disjunctive is particular; the conclusion becomes a positive disjunctive.

In the previous lesson, you mastered the eight conjunctions for a universal positive disjunctive in the first figure. Now the author extends this to the particular positive disjunctive, informing us that there are eight parallel conjunctions, but with a positive disjunctive conclusion. He leaves the detailed derivation to you. This lesson will guide you step by step through the process of deriving these eight conjunctions by analogy with the universal case.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight universal conjunctions
Write down the eight valid combinations of a universal positive disjunctive major (e.g., 'Every S is either P or Q') with a categorical minor in the first figure, as covered in the previous lesson.
These eight consist of four types of categorical minor (A, E, I, O) applied to each of the two disjuncts (P and Q).
2
Replace the universal major with a particular one
Change the major premise from 'Every S is either P or Q' to 'Some S is either P or Q'. Now the disjunctive only covers a part of S.
The minor premise remains the same categorical proposition about one of the disjuncts.
3
Adjust the conclusion to a positive disjunctive
The text says the conclusion is now a positive disjunctive. So instead of a categorical conclusion, you need to produce a disjunction. For each universal conjunction, determine the appropriate positive disjunctive conclusion that follows from the particular major and the given minor.
For example, if the minor is 'All S is P', the universal conclusion was 'No S is Q'. For the particular major, can we conclude 'Some S is not Q'? No, because the conclusion must be disjunctive. Perhaps 'Some S is P or some S is not Q'? Think systematically.
4
Verify validity for each case
Check each derived conjunction for logical validity: the conclusion must follow from the premises in the first figure. The minor must be about the term that appears in the disjunction.
The author assures us that all eight are valid, so you are confirming this.
5
Compile your list of eight
Write down all eight conjunctions in a table, showing the minor premise and the resulting positive disjunctive conclusion. Compare them with the universal list to see the parallelism.
This exercise deepens your understanding of how quantity affects disjunctive syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحملية اقترانات أخرى ثمانية موازية لهذه الاقترانات إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if the disjunctive is particular positive, then there may be combined from it with the categorical other eight conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a positive disjunctive, so consider them by yourself.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This brief passage introduces the case where the disjunctive premise is particular positive (juz'iyya mūjiba). The author informs us that there are eight additional productive conjunctions (iqtiranāt) that parallel the eight already listed for the universal positive disjunctive. The only difference is that the conclusion is also a positive disjunctive, but presumably with particular parts. The author leaves the detailed enumeration to the reader as an exercise ('so consider them by yourself'). This is a common pedagogical technique in logical texts, encouraging active engagement with the material. The reader is expected to apply the same patterns as in the universal case, adjusting the quantity of the parts.

Key Takeaway
For a particular positive disjunctive in the first figure, there are eight valid conjunctions with a categorical premise, each yielding a positive disjunctive conclusion, parallel to the universal case.

Second Figure Disjunctive Syllogisms: Conditions and First Three Conjunctions
Author introduces second figure pattern for disjunctive syllogisms, gives conditions, and enumerates first three of sixteen conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second figure disjunctive syllogism and reproduce the first three conjunction patterns from the text.

Core Idea
Second figure disjunctive syllogisms demand a universal categorical and an exclusive disjunction, yielding sixteen possible conjunctions.

In the second figure, the categorical premise shares its predicate with the disjunctive's subjects. The author specifies conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and either true or exclusive, and the categorical must be universal. He then enumerates sixteen productive conjunctions, illustrating the first three. This lesson unpacks the conditions and walks through the patterns of the first three conjunctions, highlighting the logical proofs.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the conditions
The text states three conditions: (1) the disjunctive must be affirmative, (2) it must be either true (haqiqiyya) or exclusive (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), and (3) the categorical must be universal. Note the 'aw' (or) indicates two permissible types, not optionality.
In the first figure, the disjunctive had to be both true and exclusive; here it is 'true or exclusive', a looser condition.
2
Parse the first conjunction pattern
Identify the disjunctive premise: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' (both parts universal positive). The categorical premise: 'Every A is D'. The conclusion: 'Always either some B is not A or some D is not A'.
The categorical premise's predicate (D) is the middle term. The conclusion is a disjunction of two particular negatives.
3
Trace the proof for first conjunction
The proof uses the exclusivity of the disjunction. If 'every B is J' is true, then 'not every D is J' (by exclusivity). With 'every A is J' from the categorical? Actually the categorical is 'every A is D', but the text uses 'every A is J' in the proof, indicating a possible textual corruption. Logically, if 'every A is D' and 'every D is J' (from the disjunct?), then 'every A is J'. The conclusion 'not every D is A' follows. Similarly for the other disjunct.
The proof in the text seems to have a mismatch: the categorical given is 'كل أ د' but the proof uses 'كل أ ج'. This may be a transmission error; the pattern remains valid with the correct correspondence.
4
Compare second and third conjunctions
In the second conjunction, the categorical is negative (no A is J). The conclusion becomes 'Always either no B is A or no D is A' (two universal negatives). In the third conjunction, the disjunctive parts are universal negatives (no B is J, no D is J) while the categorical is positive, yielding the same universal negative conclusion.
Notice how changing the quality of one premise switches the conclusion from particular to universal.

And as for if it is on the pattern of the second figure, and let us assume the discourse in it when the parts of the disjunction are unified in quality and quantity, then its condition is positivity of the disjunctive, and that it be true or exclusive (preventing emptiness without combination), and that the categorical be universal. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a positive disjunctive exclusive (preventing emptiness without combination). The first conjunction: from two universal positives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal positives, as we say: (Always either every B is J or every D is J) and (Every A is D). Its conclusion is (Always either some B is not A or some D is not A) because if 'Every B is J' is true, then 'Not every D is J' is true, and 'Every A is J' is true, so 'Not every D is A' is true, and if 'Every D is J' is true, then 'Not every B is J' is true, and 'Every A is J' is true, so 'Not every B is A' is true. The second conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is (It does not lack that either no B is A or no D is A) because if 'Every B is J' is true, and 'No A is J' is true, then 'No B is A' is true, and if 'Every D is J' is true, and 'No A is J' is true, then 'No D is A' is true. The third conjunction: from two universal positives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, as we say: (Always either no B is J or no D is J) and (Every A is J). Its conclusion is (Always either no B is A or no D is A) because if 'No B is J' is true, and 'Every A is J' is true, then 'No B is A' is true, and if 'No D is J' is true, and 'Every A is J' is true, then 'No D is A' is true.

Commentary
This passage introduces the second figure pattern for disjunctive syllogisms (qiyās iqṭirānī with a disjunctive premise). The author specifies that the parts of the disjunction are unified in quality and quantity. The conditions for productivity are: the disjunctive must be affirmative and either true or exclusive (here 'aw' indicates two possibilities), and the categorical must be universal. There are sixteen productive conjunctions, all yielding a positive exclusive disjunctive conclusion. The first conjunction is illustrated: both premises universal positive, disjunctive parts universal positive, e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Every A is D'. The conclusion is 'Always either some B is not A or some D is not A'. The proof relies on the exclusive nature of the disjunction: if every B is J, then not every D is J, and given that every A is J? Actually the text seems to have a mismatch: the categorical premise says 'Every A is D' but the proof uses 'Every A is J'. This suggests the example may have been corrupted in transmission. Nevertheless, the logical pattern is clear: the categorical premise's predicate is the middle term. The second conjunction is similar with a negative categorical, and the third conjunction uses a disjunctive with universal negative parts. The enumeration continues with more conjunctions in the original text. This demonstrates the systematic approach to syllogistic forms in classical logic.

Conditions and First Four Conjunctions of Second Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates first four conjunctions of second figure mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid second figure mixed syllogism and derive the conclusion for the first four conjunctions.

Core Idea
The conclusion of a second figure mixed syllogism is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction, but its form depends on the categorical premise.

In classical Islamic logic, the second figure mixed syllogism combines a disjunctive premise with a categorical premise. The passage before us lays out the conditions and enumerates productive conjunctions. We will focus on the general rule and the first four conjunctions, which illustrate the core patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
The author stipulates that the disjunctive must be affirmative (positive) and either real (haqīqiyya) or exclusive (māni'at al-khulū dūn al-jam'), and the categorical must be universal. This restricts valid premises.
These conditions ensure the conjunction is productive.
2
Analyze the first conjunction
Both premises are universal affirmative, and the disjuncts are universal affirmatives. The reasoning: if one disjunct holds, the other is false, and via the categorical we derive a negative particular conclusion. The conclusion is 'Always either some B is not A or some D is not A'.
The conclusion is a particular exclusive disjunction.
3
Examine the second conjunction
Same as the first but the categorical is negative. The conclusion becomes a universal negative exclusive disjunction: 'It cannot be that either no B is A or no D is A'.
The negative categorical flips the conclusion to universal.
4
Compare the third and fourth conjunctions
Here the disjuncts are universal negatives. For the third (categorical affirmative), conclusion is universal negative. For the fourth (categorical negative), conclusion is particular affirmative.
The interaction of categorical quality and disjunct quality determines the form.

الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية فيه سالبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ") لأنه إن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") صدق (بعض "د" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وإن صدق (لا شيء من "د" "ج") صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج").
الاقتران الخامس من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ج") ونتيجته كالاقتران الثالث؛ لأنه إن صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") صدق (لا شيء من "د" "ج") وإن صدق (بعض "د" "ج") صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ج").
الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية سالبة، ونتيجته كالاقتران الرابع، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ").
الاقتران السابع من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "د" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ج") فـ (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "د" "أ").
الاقتران الثامن الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْلية فيه سالبة، ونتيجته كالاقتران الثاني؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "ب" "ج") صدق (كل "د" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وإن صدق (كل "د" "ج") صدق (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج"). وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْلية اقترانات أخرى ثمانية، إلا أن النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا جزئية منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if it is according to the pattern of the second figure, let us assume discourse concerning it when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity. Its condition is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be real or exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khulū dūn al-jam'), and that the categorical be universal. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative exclusive disjunctive conclusion. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, such as our saying (Always either every B is J or every D is J) and (Every A is D), and its conclusion is (Always either some B is not A or some D is not A) because if (Every B is J) is true, (Not every D is J) is true, and (Every A is J) holds, so (Not every D is A); and if (Every D is J) is true, (Not every B is J) is true, and (Every A is J) holds, so (Not every B is A). The second conjunction: the conjunction remains as it is except that the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is (It cannot be that either no B is A or no D is A) because if (Every B is J) is true and (No A is J) holds, then (No B is A); and if (Every D is J) is true and (No A is J) holds, then (No D is A). The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, such as our saying: (Always either no B is J or no D is J) and (Every A is J), and its conclusion is (Always either no B is A or no D is A) because if (No B is J) is true and (Every A is J) holds, then (No B is A); and if (No D is J) is true and (Every A is J) holds, then (No D is A). The fourth conjunction: the conjunction remains as it is, except that the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is (Always either some B is not A or some D is not A) because if (No B is J) is true, (Some D is J) is true, and (No A is J) holds, and if (No D is J) is true, (Some B is J) is true, and (No A is J) holds. The fifth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, such as our saying: (Always either some B is J or some D is J) and (Every A is J), and its conclusion is like the third conjunction; because if (Some B is J) is true, (No D is J) is true, and if (Some D is J) is true, (No B is J) is true, and (Every A is J) holds. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction remains as it is, except that the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is like the fourth conjunction, namely (Always either some B is not A or some D is not A). The seventh conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, such as our saying: (Always either not every B is J or not every D is J) and (Every A is J); then (Always either not every B is A or not every D is A). The eighth conjunction: the conjunction remains as it is, except that the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is like the second conjunction; because if (Not every B is J) is true, (Every D is J) is true, and (No A is J) holds, and if (Every D is J) is true, (Every B is J) is true, and (No A is J) holds. And if the disjunctive is particular, then from it with the categorical, eight other conjunctions can be composed, except that the conclusion in them is only a particular disjunctive; so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage enumerates the productive conjunctions (iqṭirānāt) of the second figure when a disjunctive premise (al-munfaṣila) combines with a categorical premise (al-ḥamliyya). The author sets three conditions: (1) the disjunctive must be affirmative, (2) it must be either a real disjunctive (ḥaqīqiyya) or an exclusive disjunction (māni'at al-khulū dūn al-jam'), and (3) the categorical must be universal. The resulting conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction. The author then lists eight conjunctions, grouping them by the quality and quantity of the parts of the disjunction and the categorical. For example, the first conjunction has both premises universal affirmative, with the disjuncts being universal affirmatives; the conclusion is a particular exclusive disjunction. The reasoning is demonstrated through a conditional proof: if the first disjunct holds, then the second is negated, and via the categorical premise a negative conclusion follows. Each conjunction is paired with a variant where the categorical is negative, yielding a different conclusion. After the eighth, the author notes that if the disjunctive is particular, there are eight more conjunctions with particular disjunctive conclusions, leaving them as an exercise.

منفصلة
حملیة
اقتران
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

Checkpoint
In the first conjunction of the second figure mixed syllogism, if the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and the categorical is 'Every A is D', what is the conclusion?
A) Always either every B is A or every D is A
B) Always either some B is not A or some D is not A
C) Always either no B is A or no D is A
D) Always either some B is A or some D is A

Key Takeaway
In second figure mixed syllogisms, the conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction, and its form depends on the quality of the categorical premise and the disjuncts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Using the first conjunction as a model, construct a valid second figure mixed syllogism on a fiqh topic. Assume the disjunctive is 'Always either every transaction is permissible or every transaction is prohibited' and the categorical is 'Every contract is a transaction'. What is the conclusion? Check if it matches the form given.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Conjunctions Five to Eight and Particular Disjunctives
Author enumerates the fifth to eighth conjunctions and notes the particular disjunctive case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion for conjunctions 5-8 of second figure mixed syllogisms and explain the modification when the disjunctive is particular.

Core Idea
When the disjuncts are particular or negative, the conclusions mirror earlier patterns but the quality and quantity shift accordingly.

Having covered the first four conjunctions, we now turn to the remaining ones where the disjuncts are particular or the universal disjunctive has particular disjuncts. The author also notes a special case: when the disjunctive itself is particular, eight additional conjunctions yield only particular conclusions. Let's walk through them.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the fifth conjunction
The disjuncts are particular affirmatives (some B is J, some D is J) and the categorical is universal affirmative. The conclusion is like the third conjunction: a universal negative exclusive disjunction (no B A or no D A). This is because if some B is J, then no D is J, and vice versa, and the categorical gives a universal negative.
Even though disjuncts are particular, the conclusion can be universal due to the exclusive nature of the disjunction.
2
Examine the sixth conjunction
Same as fifth but the categorical is negative. The conclusion becomes a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction (some B not A or some D not A), matching the fourth conjunction.
The negative categorical flips the conclusion to particular.
3
Compare the seventh conjunction
The disjuncts are particular negatives (not every B is J, not every D is J) and the categorical is universal affirmative. The conclusion is a particular negative exclusive disjunction (not every B is A or not every D is A).
The conclusion is also particular negative.
4
Understand the eighth conjunction and the special case
The eighth conjunction uses the same disjuncts but a negative categorical, and the conclusion becomes a universal negative exclusive disjunction (no B A or no D A). After these eight, the author notes that if the disjunctive itself is particular (i.e., the whole disjunctive is particular, not its parts), then there are eight more conjunctions but the conclusions are only particular disjunctives. The reader is left to derive them.
The shift to a particular disjunctive changes the force of the conclusion.

Mixed Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms (Iqtiranat 4-8)
Enumeration of iqtiranat 4-8 of the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct each of the four mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms (iqtiranat 4-8) of the second figure by specifying the form of the disjunctive and categorical premises and deriving the valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Mixed conjunctions combine a disjunctive with a categorical to yield a disjunctive conclusion.

In this passage, the author presents four mixed conjunctions (iqtiranat) of the second figure, numbered four through eight. Each has a specific combination of a disjunctive major (exclusive or inclusive) and a categorical minor premise. Understanding these patterns is essential for evaluating arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh where disjunctive conditions arise, such as 'Either the world is created or eternal, and every created thing has a beginning.'

Guided Path
1
Recognize the overall structure
Each iqtiran consists of a disjunctive major premise (with two parts: left and right) and a categorical minor premise (either universal affirmative or universal negative). The conclusion is always a disjunction formed from the terms A (middle), B, and D. Identify the middle term C in the categorical and the disjuncts.
The second figure places the middle term as predicate of both premises.
2
Analyze the fourth iqtiran
The categorical is universal negative ('No A is C'), the disjunctive is 'Either some B is C or some D is C'. The reasoning: if one part of the disjunctive is true and C is absent from A, then the conclusion 'Either some B is not A or some D is not A' follows. Write out the two conditional proofs.
3
Analyze the fifth and sixth iqtiranat
These use a universal affirmative or negative categorical with a disjunctive whose parts are particular affirmatives (fifth) or particular negatives (sixth). The conclusions mirror those of earlier iqtiranat (third and fourth). Check how the quality of the categorical affects the conclusion.
The fifth uses 'Every A is C' and disjunctive 'Some B is C or some D is C'; the sixth uses 'No A is C' with the same disjunctive.
4
Analyze the seventh and eighth iqtiranat
Here the disjunctive parts are particular negatives ('Not every B is C' or 'Not every D is C'). With a universal affirmative categorical, the conclusion is a disjunction of particular negatives. With a universal negative categorical, the conclusion follows the pattern of the second iqtiran.
The eighth iqtiran: categorical is 'No A is C', disjunctive 'Either not every B is C or not every D is C'.
5
Generalize the pattern
Notice that when the categorical is negative, the conclusion becomes negative; when the disjunctive parts are particular, the conclusion is particular. The author adds that if the disjunctive itself is particular, eight more conjunctions exist with particular disjunctive conclusions. Work through one example as an exercise.
This pattern is a memorization aid for medieval logicians, but the underlying logical structure is what matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except that the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is (always either some 'B' is not 'A' or some 'D' is not 'A') because if 'No 'B' is 'C'' is true, then 'Some 'D' is 'C'' is true and 'No 'A' is 'C'' is true, and if 'No 'D' is 'C'' is true, then 'Some 'B' is 'C'' is true and 'No 'A' is 'C'' is true. The fifth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, as we say: (Always either some 'B' is 'C' or some 'D' is 'C') and (Every 'A' is 'C'), and its conclusion is like the third conjunction; because if 'Some 'B' is 'C'' is true, then 'No 'D' is 'C'' is true, and if 'Some 'D' is 'C'' is true, then 'No 'B' is 'C'' is true, and 'Every 'A' is 'C'' is true. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except that the categorical is negative, and its conclusion is like the fourth conjunction, and it is always either some 'B' is not 'A' or some 'D' is not 'A'. The seventh conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, as we say: (Always either not every 'B' is 'C' or not every 'D' is 'C') and (Every 'A' is 'C'), so (Always either not every 'B' is 'A' or not every 'D' is 'A'). The eighth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except that the categorical in it is negative, and its conclusion is like the second conjunction; because if 'Not every 'B' is 'C'' is true, then 'Every 'D' is 'C'' is true, and 'No 'A' is 'C'' is true, and if 'Every 'D' is 'C'' is true, then 'Every 'B' is 'C'' is true, and 'No 'A' is 'C'' is true. And if the disjunctive is particular, then from it with the categorical there may be composed other eight conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is only a particular disjunctive, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage defines four mixed conjunctions (iqtiranat) of the second figure, where the major premise is a disjunctive (either exclusive or inclusive) and the minor is a categorical. The second figure places the middle term as predicate of both premises. The author numbers them from fourth to eighth. For each, he states the form, the condition, and the conclusion along with the reasoning. The fourth conjunction has a universal negative categorical, yielding a particular negative disjunctive conclusion. The fifth uses two universal affirmatives with a particular affirmative disjunctive, and the conclusion follows the pattern of the third conjunction (from earlier). The sixth is like the fourth but with a negative categorical again; the seventh uses particular negative parts in the disjunctive with a universal affirmative categorical; the eighth has a universal negative categorical and yields as in the second conjunction. Finally, the author notes that if the disjunctive is particular, there are eight more conjunctions, but the conclusion is always a particular disjunctive. This is a technical enumeration meant for memorization; the student should work out the remaining eight as an exercise.

حمْلية
منفصلة

Checkpoint
In the fourth iqtiran of the second figure, what is the conclusion if the categorical is 'No A is C' and the disjunctive is 'Either some B is C or some D is C'?
A) Either some B is not A or some D is not A
B) Either every B is A or every D is A
C) Either some B is A or some D is A
D) Either not every B is A or not every D is A

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms yield disjunctive conclusions, and the validity depends on the categorical premise's quality and the disjuncts' quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the fourth iqtiran: disjunctive 'Either some created things are eternal or some created things are temporal' and categorical 'No uncaused thing is temporal.' Write the correct conclusion in the form 'Either some [B] is not [A] or some [D] is not [A]' and test the conditions.

2. Construct the seventh iqtiran using a fiqh example: 'Every forbidden thing is harmful', and 'Either not every intoxicant is forbidden or not every intoxicant is harmful.' What is the conclusion? Check your reasoning against the author's pattern.

The Syllogism

General Conditions for Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms
General conditions for third figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid third figure mixed syllogism and identify the allowed types of disjunction.

Core Idea
Third figure mixed syllogisms require a true exclusive or inclusive-of-empty disjunction and a universal categorical.

This lesson introduces the general rules for constructing valid mixed syllogisms in the third figure. These rules govern which disjunctions and categoricals can combine to yield a productive argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third figure structure
Recognize that the middle term is the subject of both premises.
2
Check the disjunctive premise conditions
The disjunctive must be affirmative and either haqiqiyya (true exclusive) or man'at al-khuluw (inclusive of empty but not of conjunction).
3
Ensure the categorical premise is universal
The categorical must be universal in quantity.
If the categorical is particular, it may not produce a valid conclusion.
4
Understand the conclusion form
The conclusion is a conditional (muttasila) whose antecedent is the part of the disjunction not shared with the categorical.
5
Note the number of productive conjunctions
There are 24 productive conjunctions when the disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives.
This is a general count; the enumeration begins with the first nine.

And if it is of the pattern of the third figure, let us also suppose the discourse in it when the disjunctive is uniform in quantity and quality—and you know that in the mixed type—and its condition is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be true exclusive or inclusive of the empty but not of the conjunction, and that the categorical be universal, and if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four, and especially that the conclusion in it is a conditional whose antecedent is exactly the part that has no sharing from the parts of the disjunction, and if the parts of the disjunctive are affirmative or its opposite, if the parts are negative, its consequent is the result of the conjunction of the other part of the parts of the disjunctive with the categorical. Commentary
This passage begins the enumeration of the third figure's mixed conjunctions (iqtiranat). The author first states the general conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and true exclusive (haqiqiyya) or inclusive of the empty but not of the conjunction; the categorical must be universal. If the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, there are 24 productive conjunctions. The conclusion of third figure conjunctions is a conditional (muttasila) whose antecedent is the part of the disjunction not shared with the categorical. Then the author lists the first nine conjunctions. For each, he gives the premise forms and the conclusion. The first conjunction has a universal affirmative disjunctive and a universal affirmative categorical (with the minor term 'B' as subject of the categorical, note the middle term is 'C'? Actually the categorical is 'Every B is A'? Let's check: The example says (كل "ب" "أ") so the categorical is 'Every B is A' and the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is C or every D is C'. The middle term appears to be C? This is tricky. In third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises. Here the categorical has B as subject, and the disjunctive has B and D in the parts. The reasoning given: if it is not that every D is C, then every B is C, and since every B is A, then some C is A. The conclusion is a conditional: If not every D is B, then some C is A. Wait, the Arabic says 'إن كان ليس كل "د" "ب" فبعض "ج" "أ"' so the antecedent is 'not every D is B'? That seems odd; maybe it's a typo? Possibly it should be 'not every D is C'. But the author writes 'ليس كل "د" "ب"'. We must follow the text. In the reasoning, they use if not every D is C, so there is inconsistency. We'll note it as is. The following conjunctions follow similar patterns, varying the quantity and quality of the categorical premise and the disjunctive parts. The ninth iqtiran is cut off mid-sentence.

The First Nine Productive Conjunctions (Iqtiranat) of the Third Figure
Enumeration of first nine iqtiranat of the third figure with premises and conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion for any of the first nine iqtiranat of the third figure given the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion always follows a pattern: if the categorical negates the shared part, then the other part yields a sub-conclusion.

In this lesson, we walk through the first nine valid conjunctions of the third figure. For each, the premises are given, and the conclusion is derived. Understanding these patterns helps in constructing similar arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises for each iqtiran
For each conjunction, note the form of the disjunctive (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and the categorical (universal/particular, affirmative/negative).
2
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: its antecedent is the negation of one part of the disjunction; its consequent is derived from the other part and the categorical.
The antecedent is always from the part that is 'not shared' with the categorical.
3
Verify the reasoning
The text provides a short proof for each: assuming the antecedent true, derive the consequent using the disjunctive and categorical.
The proofs use immediate inferences from the categorical to convert terms.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Substitute variables with Islamic content, e.g., B=nabidh, C=intoxicant, A=haram.
This shows the logical form is content-independent.

If the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, there are 24 productive conjunctions. The conclusion of third figure conjunctions is a conditional (muttasila) whose antecedent is the part of the disjunction not shared with the categorical. Then the author lists the first nine conjunctions. For each, he gives the premise forms and the conclusion. The first conjunction has a universal affirmative disjunctive and a universal affirmative categorical (with the minor term 'B' as subject of the categorical, note the middle term is 'C'? The middle term appears to be C? This is tricky. In third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises. Here the categorical has B as subject, and the disjunctive has B and D in the parts. Conjunctive Syllogisms with Universal Positive Disjunction
Enumeration of twelve conjunctions with universal positive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conditional conclusion of any conjunctive syllogism given a universal positive disjunction and a categorical premise of known quantity and quality.

Core Idea
The twelve conjunctions are variations on denying one disjunct and applying the categorical to the other.

The author systematically lists all combinations of a universal positive disjunction with a categorical premise. We will focus on the first conjunction to understand the logical engine, then see how changes in quantity and quality produce different conclusions. This pattern underpins many inferential forms in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction and categorical premise
In the first conjunction, the disjunction is 'always either every B is J or every D is J' (universal positive). The categorical premise is 'every B is A' (universal positive). Write both down.
B, D, J, A are placeholders for terms in Islamic sciences.
2
Negate one disjunct conditionally
Assume the negation of one part of the disjunction: 'if it is not the case that every D is J'. This forces the other disjunct 'every B is J' to hold, because the disjunction is inclusive and universal.
The disjunction is 'always either ... or ...', so at least one universal claim holds in every case.
3
Apply the categorical premise
From 'every B is J' (now true) and 'every B is A' (the categorical premise), deduce by first-figure categorical syllogism that 'some J is A' (since the middle term B is shared).
This is a standard Barbara-like inference: all B are J, all B are A, therefore some J are A (by subalternation or conversion).
4
Formulate the conditional conclusion
Combine the assumption and result: 'If not every D is J, then some J is A'. This is the conclusion of the first conjunction. The source writes 'if not every D is B', but logically it should be 'if not every D is J' based on the reasoning.
The pattern: the antecedent negates one disjunct, and the consequent follows from the other disjunct combined with the categorical.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the moods of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) when the disjunctive premise is universal positive. The author presents twelve conjunctions (iqtiran) that vary based on the quantity and quality of the categorical premise (حملیة) and the parts of the disjunction (أجزاء المنفصلة). Each conjunction specifies the combination of premises and the resulting conditional conclusion. The first conjunction, for example, takes a universal positive disjunction (e.g., 'Always, either every B is J or every D is J') and a universal positive categorical (e.g., 'Every B is A'), and yields the conclusion: 'If not every D is B, then some J is A.' The reasoning is that if 'not every D is J' holds, then 'every B is J' must hold (by the disjunction), and since 'every B is A', it follows that some J is A. The subsequent conjunctions modify the categorical or the disjunction's parts. The pattern is consistent: the categorical's quantity and quality affect the conclusion's form. For a fiqh application, let B stand for 'khamr' (intoxicant), D for 'nabidh', J for 'forbidden', and A for 'wine'. Then the disjunction 'Every khamr is forbidden or every nabidh is forbidden' and the categorical 'Every khamr is wine' yield 'If not every nabidh is khamr, then some forbidden things are wine.' This illustrates how the logical forms can be mapped to Islamic legal reasoning.

The Fourth Figure of Conjunctive Syllogisms: Conditions and First Three Moods
Conditions and first three moods of fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a productive fourth-figure conjunctive syllogism and evaluate the first three moods using the given parameters.

Core Idea
Fourth figure conjunctions yield a conditional conclusion from a disjunction and a categorical premise.

This passage expands on the fourth figure of conjunctive syllogisms, specifying when a conjunction of a disjunctive and a categorical premise yields a productive argument. The author sets conditions on the disjunction and the categorical, then demonstrates three specific moods with variables. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid compound syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when reasoning about legal or theological disjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall form
The passage describes a fourth-figure conjunctive syllogism: a disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Either every B is J or every D is J') combined with a categorical premise (e.g., 'Every A is B') produces a conditional conclusion ('If not every D is J, then some J is A'). Recognize that the conclusion is always a conditional (natija muttasila).
2
List the conditions for productivity
The disjunction must be affirmative and either real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without commonality (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'). The categorical cannot be particular negative; if it is particular positive, the disjunction's parts must be universal positives or particular negatives.
These conditions ensure that the syllogism is valid.
3
Parse the first mood
First mood: disjunction (universal positive parts) and categorical universal positive. Example: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Every A is B'. Conclusion: 'If it is not that every D is J, then some J is A'. Trace the reasoning: if not every D is J, then every B is J (from disjunction), and since every A is B, we get some J is A.
The part with no commonality is D (does not appear in categorical); the part with commonality is B (appears in categorical).
4
Identify variations in second and third moods
Second mood: same disjunction but categorical is universal negative. Conclusion: 'If not every D is J, then some J is not A'. Third mood: categorical is particular positive; conclusion same as first mood but from different premises.
Note that the antecedent remains the same; only the consequent changes based on the categorical.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Let A = khamr, B = intoxicant, D = nabidh, J = forbidden. The disjunction 'Every intoxicant is forbidden or every nabidh is forbidden' and 'Every khamr is an intoxicant' yields 'If not every nabidh is forbidden, then some forbidden things are khamr'. Check that conditions hold: disjunction is real (true) and categorical is universal positive.
This demonstrates how the logical form applies to fiqh reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الرابع، ولنفرض الكلام أيضاً إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن لا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليّات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة عشرون اقتراناً، ونتيجته في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها عين الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة أو نقيضه، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران الجزء الذي فيه الاشتراك بالحَمْلية الاقتران الأول: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كلية موجبة كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") لأنه إذا لم يكن (كل "د" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") وكان كل "أ" "ب") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية كلية سالبة، ونتيجته (إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة ونتيجته إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ").
And if it is according to the pattern of the fourth figure, let us also suppose if the parts of the disjunction are homogeneous in quantity and quality, and its condition for production is that the disjunction is affirmative, and that it is real or exclusive of emptiness without commonality, and that the categorical is not particular negative; and if the categorical is particular positive, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal positives or particular negatives. Its productive indicators are twenty conjunctions, and its conclusion in all is a positive conditional, its antecedent is the same as the part that has no commonality in the parts of the disjunction or its contradictory, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the part that has commonality with the categorical. The first conjunction: from two universal positives and the parts of the disjunction are universal positive, as our saying: (Always either every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'J') and (every 'A' is 'B'), and its conclusion: (If it is not that every 'D' is 'J') then (some 'J' is 'A') because if it is not that (every 'D' is 'J') then (every 'B' is 'J') and (every 'A' is 'B') was true, so (some 'J' is 'A'). The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion: (If it is not that every 'D' is 'J') then (some 'J' is not 'A'). Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع) of conjunctive syllogisms. The author sets the conditions for productive conjunctions: the disjunction must be affirmative and either real (حقيقية) or exclusive of emptiness without commonality (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), meaning a genuine exclusive disjunction. The categorical premise cannot be particular negative; if it is particular positive, the disjunction's parts must be either universal positives or particular negatives. There are twenty such conjunctions. The conclusion is always a positive conditional (موجبة متصلة), with the antecedent being the part of the disjunction that has no commonality (i.e., that shares no term with the categorical) or its contradictory, and the consequent being the result of combining the part with commonality with the categorical. Three examples are given. In the first, the disjunction 'Either every B is J or every D is J' and categorical 'Every A is B' yield: 'If not every D is J, then some J is A'. The reasoning: if 'not every D is J', then 'every B is J' (by the disjunction), and since 'every A is B', we get 'some J is A' (since some B is J and every A is B). For an Islamic example, let A be 'khamr', B be 'intoxicant', D be 'nabidh', J be 'forbidden'. Then the disjunction 'Every intoxicant is forbidden or every nabidh is forbidden' and 'Every khamr is an intoxicant' yield 'If not every nabidh is forbidden, then some forbidden things are khamr'. The second and third moods vary the categorical to universal negative and particular positive respectively.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
نتيجة متصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It must be universal and exclusive of commonality

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure of conjunctive syllogisms requires an affirmative disjunction (real or of the mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam' type) and yields a conditional conclusion; the first three moods illustrate how the categorical premise affects the consequent.

Fourth Figure Compound Syllogisms: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author enumerates conditions and moods for fourth figure compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid fourth figure compound syllogism and construct its conclusion in conditional form.

Core Idea
Fourth figure compound syllogisms require a true exclusive disjunction and specific restrictions on the categorical premise.

This lesson examines compound syllogisms where one premise is a disjunction and the other is a categorical, arranged in the fourth figure. The author lays out strict conditions for productivity and provides numerous moods. Understanding these will help you analyze complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where moral or theological conclusions are drawn from disjunctive premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunctive (منفصلة) in the syllogism. Check that it is affirmative and either a true exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) or a disjunction that prevents emptiness but not conjunction.
The disjunction must be affirmative, not negative.
2
Examine the categorical premise
Check the categorical (حمْلية) for its quantity and quality. It must not be particular negative. If it is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives.
The restriction ensures the syllogism produces a valid conclusion.
3
Determine the conclusion's structure
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional. Its antecedent is the part of the disjunction that does not share a term with the categorical (or its negation), and its consequent is the result of combining the shared part with the categorical.
The conclusion is a conditional, not a categorical proposition.
4
Map a mood to its variables
Take the first mood: disjunction 'Always, either every B is C or every D is C' and categorical 'Every A is B'. The shared term is B. The conclusion is 'If not every D is C, then some C is A'.
The antecedent uses the part without B (D-C) negated; the consequent uses the shared term B linking A to C.
5
Check additional moods
Practice with other moods by varying the categorical's quality and quantity or the disjunction's parts. Note that conclusions adjust accordingly.
Twelve moods are listed; another twelve exist when the disjunction is particular affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليّات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب. وقرائنه المنتجة عشرون اقتراناً، ونتيجته في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها عين الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة أو نقيضه، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران الجزء الذي فيه الاشتراك بالحَمْلية. الاقتران الأول: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية كلية سالبة، ونتيجته: (إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته: (إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الرابع: من كليتين موجبتين، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الخامس: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية كليّة سالبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران السادس: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" "[؟]"). الاقتران السابع: من كليتين موجبتين، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، ونتيجته: (إن كان بعض "د" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثامن: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية كلية سالبة، ونتيجته: إن لم يكن بعض "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "[؟]"). الاقتران التاسع: من كليتين موجبتين إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سوالب، ونتيجته: (إن كان بعض "د" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ"). الاقتران العاشر: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية كليّة سالبة، ونتيجته: إن كان لا شيء من "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الحادي عشر: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني عشر: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْلية فيه جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج" فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحملية اثنا عشر اقتراناً أخرى موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة متصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك. وأما إن كانت الحَمْلية متكثرة والمنفصلة بحالها فهيئة الحد الأوسط لا تخرج عن الأشكال الأربعة، فإن كان على نسق الشكل الأول فشرطه إيجاب المنفصلة.
And as for when it is in the pattern of the fourth figure, let us also assume that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality. Its condition for production is that the disjunctive be affirmative, that it be true exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) and not (مانعة الجمع), and that the categorical not be particular negative; and if the categorical is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Its productive moods are twenty moods, and its conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the very part of the disjunctive that has no commonality (with the categorical) or its contradictory, and whose consequent is the result of combining the part that has commonality with the categorical. The first mood: from two universal positives with the parts of the disjunction universal positive, like saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every D is C' and 'Every A is B'. Its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is A'. The second mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is not A'. The third mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is particular positive, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is A'. The fourth mood: from two universal positives, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is A'. The fifth mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is not A'. The sixth mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is particular positive, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is [؟]'. The seventh mood: from two universal positives, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular positives, and its conclusion: 'If some D is C, then no C is A'. The eighth mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion: 'If not some D is C, then some C is not [؟]'. The ninth mood: from two universal positives, except that the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, and its conclusion: 'If some D is C, then no C is A'. The tenth mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion: 'If no D is C, then some C is not A'. The eleventh mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is particular positive, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is A'. The twelfth mood: the same mood, except that the categorical is particular negative, and its conclusion: 'If not every D is C, then some C is not A'. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may be composed with the categorical another twelve moods parallel to these moods, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative conditional, so consider them yourself. And as for when the categorical is multiplied and the disjunctive remains the same, the form of the middle term does not leave the four figures; if it is in the pattern of the first figure, then its condition is the affirmation of the disjunctive.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of compound syllogisms in the fourth figure, where one premise is a disjunction (منفصلة) and the other is a categorical (حمْلية). The author first states the conditions for the fourth figure to be productive: the disjunction must be affirmative and must be either a true exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) or a disjunction that prevents emptiness without preventing collection, and the categorical cannot be particular negative. If the categorical is particular affirmative, then the disjunction's parts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. There are twenty productive moods listed, and the conclusion is always an affirmative conditional. The conclusion's antecedent is a part of the disjunction that does not share a term with the categorical (or its negation), and the consequent is the result of combining the shared part with the categorical. The author then enumerates twelve specific moods with examples using placeholders (أ, ب, ج, د). For instance, the first mood has a universal positive disjunction and a universal positive categorical, yielding a conditional conclusion: if not every D is C, then some C is A. The second mood changes the categorical to universal negative, giving a different conditional. The pattern continues through the twelfth mood. After this, the author notes that if the disjunction is particular affirmative, there are another twelve parallel moods with particular affirmative conclusions. Finally, if the categorical is multiplied (i.e., more than one categorical premise), the middle term will follow one of the four figures; if it is in the first figure, the condition remains that the disjunction must be affirmative. All examples use variables representing terms from the Islamic sciences, such as A, B, C, D as placeholders for logical terms.

Key Terms
منفصلة
حمْلية
اقتران
شكل رابع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required of the disjunctive premise in the fourth figure compound syllogism for it to produce a conclusion?
A) It must be negative and inclusive.
B) It must be affirmative and either true exclusive or مانعة الخلو.
C) It must be particular affirmative.
D) It must be universal negative.

Key Takeaway
Fourth figure compound syllogisms are valid only when the disjunctive premise is affirmative and the categorical premise meets specific quantity/quality restrictions, producing a conditional conclusion whose antecedent and consequent are derived from the disjunction's parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the disjunctive premise 'Either every human action is created by Allah or every human action is acquired by humans' and the categorical 'Every good action is acquired', determine the conclusion using the first mood conditions and write it as a conditional.
?
Analyze the following argument: 'Always, either every created thing is temporally originated or every contingent thing is temporally originated. No soul is a created thing.' Identify which mood this corresponds to and state the conclusion, checking that the conditions are satisfied.

The Fourth Conjunction: A Model for Conjunctive Forms
Author details the fourth conjunctive form with justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical derivation of the fourth conjunctive form and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The fourth conjunction shows how a disjunctive premise with negative parts and a categorical premise yield a valid conditional conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the first of the ten conjunctive forms described by the author. The fourth conjunction combines two affirmative categorical premises with a disjunctive whose parts are particular negatives. The author derives a conditional conclusion and provides a brief justification. We will walk through the steps of this derivation and see how the categorical sub-syllogisms support the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The author states: 'from two affirmative categoricals, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives.' The disjunctive parts are 'not every D is J' (a particular negative) and implicitly 'every B is J' (a universal affirmative). The categorical premise is 'every A is B' (universal affirmative).
The disjunctive is not explicitly written, but the justification reveals the second part.
2
Apply the disjunctive syllogism
Assume the antecedent of the conclusion: 'if it is not that every D is J'. From the disjunctive, if one part is false, the other is true. The author says 'because if it is not that every D is J, then every B is J.' So we deduce 'every B is J'.
The disjunctive is likely exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), but the author does not specify.
3
Form a categorical syllogism
We have 'every A is B' and 'every B is J'. This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism, concluding 'every A is J'.
The author implicitly uses this step.
4
Convert the conclusion
From 'every A is J', we can convert per accidens to 'some J is A'. This matches the conclusion 'some J is A' in the conditional.
The conversion is valid only if A is not empty, but this is assumed in traditional logic.
5
Form the conditional
The overall conclusion is the conditional: 'if it is not that every D is J, then some J is A'. The derivation shows that if the antecedent holds, the consequent follows.
This conditional is the conclusion of the iqtiran.

الاقتران الخامس بحاله، إلا أن الحمْلية كليّة سالبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ").
الاقتران السادس بحاله، إلا أن الحمْلية جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته: إن كان ليس كل "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" [؟]).
الاقتران السابع من كليتين موجبتين، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، ونتيجته: إن كان بعض "د" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ")، لأنه إذا صدق (بعض "د" "ج") صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ب") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ")، لما عرف في الحمْليات.
