الاقتران العاشر بحاله، إلا أن الحمْلية كليّة سالبة، ونتيجته: إن كان لا شيء من "د" "ج") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ")، لأنه إذا صدق (لا شيء من "د" "ج") صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ").
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تألف منها مع الحملية اقترانات أخرى عشرة موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
The fourth conjunction: from two affirmative categoricals, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, and its conclusion is: if it is not that every D is J, then some J is A, because if it is not that every D is J, then every B is J.
The fifth conjunction: the same, except that the categorical is a universal negative, and its conclusion is: if it is not that every D is J, then some J is not A.
The sixth conjunction: the same, except that the categorical is a particular affirmative, and its conclusion is: if it is not that every D is J, then some J is [؟].
The seventh conjunction: from two affirmative categoricals, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, and its conclusion is: if some D is J, then nothing of J is A, because if some D is J is true, then nothing of B is J is true, and it was that every A is B, so nothing of J is A, as known in categoricals.
The eighth conjunction: the same, except that the categorical is a universal negative, and its conclusion is: if not some D is J, then some J is not [؟], because if not some D is J, then some B is J, and it was that nothing of A is B, so some J is not [؟].
The ninth conjunction: from two affirmative categoricals, except that the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives, and its conclusion is: if some D is J, then nothing of J is A, because if some D is J is true, then nothing of B is J is true, and it was that every A is B, so nothing of J is A.
The tenth conjunction: the same, except that the categorical is a universal negative, and its conclusion is: if nothing of D is J, then some J is not A, because if nothing of D is J is true, then some B is J is true, and it was that nothing of A is B, so some J is not A.
And if the disjunctive is a particular affirmative, then with the categorical there arise ten other conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative; so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines ten specific conjunctive forms (iqṭirānāt) of compound syllogisms where the major premise is a disjunctive statement (al-munfaṣilah) and the minor is a categorical statement (al-ḥamliyyah). The author systematically lists each form from the fourth to the tenth, indicating the quantity and quality of both the disjunctive parts and the categorical premise, and then states the resulting conclusion.

The first form (fourth) assumes both categoricals are affirmative, but the disjunctive parts are particular negatives. The conclusion is a conditional: if the universal negative of the disjunct is false, then some J is A. The author provides a justification using the rules of categorical syllogisms, showing how the middle term (B) leads to the conclusion.

The fifth and sixth are variations: the fifth changes the categorical to universal negative; the sixth changes it to particular affirmative (the conclusion here is partially illegible in the source).

The seventh changes the disjunctive to particular affirmatives; the conclusion changes to a universal negative (nothing of J is A). The reasoning is explained: from 'some D is J' we deduce 'nothing of B is J' (via the disjunctive property), then with 'every A is B' we get 'nothing of J is A'.

The eighth changes the categorical to universal negative again, with the same disjunctive (particular affirmatives). The conclusion is a particular negative, though missing the subject in the source.

The ninth and tenth shift to universal negatives for the disjunctive parts. The ninth uses affirmative categoricals; the tenth uses a universal negative categorical. The conclusions are derived similarly.

Finally, the author notes that if the disjunctive is a particular affirmative, then ten parallel conjunctions arise, with the difference that the conclusions are all particular affirmative. He leaves the details to the reader, implying the pattern is consistent and can be extrapolated.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحمْلية
الاقتران
جزئيات سوالب

A) Because if it is not that every D is J, then every B is J.
B) Because if some D is J, then every B is J.
C) Because if no D is J, then some B is J.
D) Because if every D is J, then no B is J.

Key Takeaway
The fourth conjunction demonstrates that from a disjunctive premise with particular negative parts and a universal affirmative categorical, a valid conditional conclusion can be derived through a series of well-defined logical steps.

The Remaining Conjunctions (5th-10th): Variations on a Theme
Author lists the remaining six conjunctive forms (5th-10th).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the conclusion of any of the ten conjunctive forms given the types of premises.

Core Idea
The ten conjunctions are systematic variations on the fourth, changing the quality and quantity of the categorical premise and the disjunctive parts.

In this lesson, we survey the remaining six conjunctions (5th-10th) described by the author. Each is a variation of the fourth conjunction, with changes to the categorical premise or the disjunctive parts. By recognizing the pattern, you can derive the conclusions without memorizing each one individually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base pattern (4th conjunction)
The fourth conjunction: disjunctive parts are particular negatives, both categoricals are universal affirmatives, conclusion: if not every D is J, then some J is A.
This pattern serves as the template.
2
Observe the variation in the 5th and 6th conjunctions
The 5th changes the categorical to universal negative; the 6th changes to particular affirmative. The conclusion changes accordingly: 5th becomes 'some J is not A', 6th becomes 'some J is [?]' (missing term).
The author provides the conclusion formula for each.
3
Observe the variation in the 7th-10th conjunctions
The 7th-10th change the disjunctive parts to particular affirmatives (7th-8th) and universal negatives (9th-10th). The categorical also varies between affirmative and universal negative.
Use the table provided in the passage to see each conclusion's form.
4
Generalize the pattern
For any combination, the conclusion is a conditional where the antecedent is a part of the disjunctive, and the consequent is derived from the categorical and the implied other part of the disjunctive.
The author leaves the case of particular affirmative disjunctive to the reader.

The conclusion is a conditional: if the universal negative of the disjunct is false, then some J is A. The author provides a justification using the rules of categorical syllogisms, showing how the middle term (B) leads to the conclusion.

The fifth and sixth are variations: the fifth changes the categorical to universal negative; the sixth changes it to particular affirmative (the conclusion here is partially illegible in the source).

The seventh changes the disjunctive to particular affirmatives; the conclusion changes to a universal negative (nothing of J is A). The reasoning is explained: from 'some D is J' we deduce 'nothing of B is J' (via the disjunctive property), then with 'every A is B' we get 'nothing of J is A'.

The eighth changes the categorical to universal negative again, with the same disjunctive (particular affirmatives). The conclusion is a particular negative, though missing the subject in the source.

The ninth and tenth shift to universal negatives for the disjunctive parts. The ninth uses affirmative categoricals; the tenth uses a universal negative categorical. The conclusions are derived similarly.

Finally, the author notes that if the disjunctive is a particular affirmative, then ten parallel conjunctions arise, with the difference that the conclusions are all particular affirmative. He leaves the details to the reader, implying the pattern is consistent and can be extrapolated.

First Figure Compound Syllogisms: Conditions and Conjunctions
Author states conditions and gives three example conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid first figure compound syllogism with a disjunctive major and construct the three basic conjunction forms.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative disjunctive that is exclusive, and universal categoricals.

The author now extends the first figure to compound syllogisms where the major premise is disjunctive and multiple categoricals are used as minor premises. The conditions are strict: the disjunctive must be affirmative and of a specific exclusive type, and all categoricals must be universal. He then gives three example conjunctions that cover affirmative, negative, and mixed categoricals. Mastering these conditions is essential for constructing valid compound syllogisms in Islamic dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four figures of categorical syllogisms
The author notes that the middle term arrangement in compound syllogisms still falls within the four figures. For the first figure, the middle term is subject of one premise and predicate of the other.
This is the foundation for understanding the compound form.
2
Identify the conditions for the first figure compound syllogism
Find the two conditions stated: (1) the disjunctive must be affirmative, and (2) it must be either 'true exclusive' (ḥaqīqiyyah) or 'exclusive of void without union' (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘). Also, all categoricals must be universal.
These conditions ensure the validity of the conclusion.
3
Analyze the three example conjunctions
Study the three forms given: first with all universal affirmatives, second with universal negatives, third with mixed quality. Notice how the conclusion changes accordingly.
The pattern shows how the conclusion mirrors the qualities of the categoricals.
4
Recognize the systematic enumeration of further conjunctions
The author outlines how varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts leads to more conjunctions (3, 6, 12). This shows the completeness of the classification.
The reader is encouraged to derive these independently.

And if the categorical is multiple and the disjunctive as it is, then the form of the middle term does not leave the four figures. Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and forms of compound syllogisms when the categorical premise is multiple (i.e., more than one) and the disjunctive remains as the major premise. The author states that the middle term arrangement still falls within the four figures of categorical syllogisms. Specifically, for the first figure, the conditions are: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and it must be either 'true exclusive' (ḥaqīqiyyah) or 'exclusive of void without union' (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘). The categoricals must all be universal. Under these conditions, there are twenty-four productive conjunctions, all yielding an affirmative disjunctive conclusion that is exclusive of void without union.

The author then gives three examples of such conjunctions. The first uses all universal affirmative propositions: a disjunctive (either every B is J or every D is J) and two categoricals (every J is something? and every J is R). The conclusion is a disjunction: either every B is A and R, or every D is A and R (with some missing terms in the original). The second variation uses universal negatives for the categoricals, changing the conclusion to negative disjuncts. The third uses mixed quality categoricals (one affirmative, one negative), resulting in a mixed conclusion.

After these examples, the author outlines other possibilities based on the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts. If the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, three parallel conjunctions arise (left for the reader). If the parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, six conjunctions arise with particular conclusions. If the entire disjunctive is a particular affirmative, twelve conjunctions arise with particular affirmative disjunctive conclusions. This systematic enumeration shows the logical completeness of the classification.

Second Figure Compound Syllogisms: Conditions and First Conjunction
Author states conditions for second figure compound syllogisms and gives the first example conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second figure compound syllogism and derive the conclusion of the first conjunction.

Core Idea
Second figure compound syllogisms require a true exclusive disjunction and uniform categoricals to produce a particular exclusive conclusion.

In this passage, the author specifies the conditions under which a compound syllogism composed of a disjunctive premise and two categorical premises, arranged in the second figure, yields a valid conclusion. He then provides the first of sixteen possible conjunctions, demonstrating how the terms combine. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic logic, especially in theological and juristic reasoning where disjunctive premises are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and structure
Recognize that the syllogism is in the second figure, meaning the middle term is predicate in both categorical premises. Also note it is compound: one disjunctive major and two categorical minors.
2
Extract the conditions
Note that the disjunctive must be affirmative and either 'true exclusive' (mani'at al-khuluw wa al-jam') or 'exclusive of void without union' (mani'at al-khuluw la al-jam'). Also the categoricals must be universal and uniform in quality.
Uniform quality means all categorical premises are either all affirmative or all negative.
3
Determine the productive conjunctions
The author states there are sixteen productive conjunctions; the conclusion in all is exclusive of void without union. This emphasizes that the disjunctive conclusion is always of the 'exclusive of void' type.
4
Analyze the first conjunction
Write out the premises and conclusion in terms of the variables B, D, J, A, R. Verify that it satisfies the conditions: disjunctive is universal affirmative exclusive, categoricals are universal affirmative. The conclusion is a particular exclusive disjunction.
The conclusion says: always either some B is not A and not R, or some D is not A and not R.

And if they are according to the second figure, then the condition for its productivity is that the disjunctive be affirmative, true exclusive or exclusive of void without union, and that the categoricals be universal and uniform in quality. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen, and the conclusion in all is exclusive of void without union. The first conjunction: from an affirmative universal disjunction whose parts are universal affirmative and categoricals universal affirmative, as if we say: always either every B is J or every D is J, and every A is J) and (every R is J), and its conclusion is (always either some B or some D is not A and not R).

Commentary
This passage presents the conditions for compound syllogisms in the second figure. The disjunctive must be affirmative and either 'true exclusive' or 'exclusive of void without union' (similar to the first figure). Additionally, the categorical premises must all be universal and uniform in quality (i.e., all affirmative or all negative). Under these conditions, there are sixteen productive conjunctions, all yielding a conclusion that is exclusive of void without union (a disjunction where at least one alternative holds, but both may hold).

The author provides one example: the first conjunction. This uses a universal affirmative disjunction (either every B is J or every D is J) and two universal affirmative categoricals (every A is J and every R is J). The conclusion is a particular negative disjunction: either some B is not A and not R, or some D is not A and not R. In other words, at least one of B or D is not both A and R. This illustrates how the second figure differs from the first: the middle term is in the predicate position of both categoricals, and the conclusion is particular rather than universal.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thānī): The second figure of categorical syllogisms, where the middle term is predicate in both premises. Here applied to compound syllogisms with a disjunctive major.
متحدة الكيفية (muttahidat al-kayfiyyah): Uniform in quality: all categorical premises must be either all affirmative or all negative.
قرائنه (qarā’inuh): The valid conjunctive forms that produce a conclusion under the given conditions.

Conditions for Productive First Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Conditions for first figure disjunctive-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive premise and categorical minors satisfy the conditions for a valid first figure mixed syllogism according to the author.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction plus universal categoricals guarantees a valid conclusion.

The author begins by laying down the essential conditions for any first figure syllogism that combines a disjunctive major with categorical minors. Without meeting these conditions, the combination will not yield a necessary conclusion. Understanding these rules is the first step to mastering the 24 productive moods enumerated later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type
Check if the disjunctive is haqiqiyya (true exclusive) or mani'at al-khuluw dun al-jam (exclusive of emptiness without exclusiveness of union). Both are exclusive disjunctions.
2
Verify categorical universality
Ensure the categorical premises (minor premises) are universal in quantity, whether affirmative or negative.
The passage states: 'وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات'.
3
Determine the conclusion form
The conclusion will always be a positive exclusive disjunction of the same type (مفصلة موجبة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع).

Commentary
This passage provides the conditions and enumerates the productive moods (iqiranat) for mixed syllogisms of the first figure, where the major premise is a disjunctive and the other premises are categorical (hamliyya). The Three Basic Combinations of First Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Three explicit examples of mixed syllogism moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three explicit combinations given by the author and derive their conclusions.

Core Idea
Three combinations of categorical quality yield three distinct conclusions: all positive, all negative, or mixed.

After stating the conditions, the author provides three concrete examples of productive moods. These illustrate how the quality of the categorical premises affects the conclusion. Mastering these three will allow you to analogously understand the remaining 21 combinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major
The major premise is a universal positive exclusive disjunction: 'دائماً إما كل ب ج أو كل د ج'.
2
Analyze the first combination
Both categoricals are universal positive: 'كل ج أ' and 'كل ج ر'. The conclusion is a universal positive disjunction: either every B is A and R or every D is A and R.
The author's wording: 'لا يخلو إما كل ب أو كل د أ وراء'.
3
Analyze the second combination
Both categoricals are universal negative: 'لا شيء من ج أ' and 'لا شيء من ج ر'. The conclusion is a universal negative disjunction: either no B is A and R or no D is A and R.
Author: 'إما لا شيء من ب أ وراء وإما لا شيء من د أ وراء'.
4
Analyze the third combination
One categorical positive, one negative: 'كل ج أ' and 'لا شيء من ج ر'. The conclusion is mixed: either every B is A and not R or every D is A and not R.
Author: 'إما كل ب أ ولا ر وإما كل د أ ولا ر'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً، ونتيجته في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وحَمْليات كليّة موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "ج" "أ" وكل "ج" "ر" ونتيجته لا يخلو إما كل "ب" وإما كل "د" "أ" و "ر". الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب، ونتيجته إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" و"ر" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ" و"ر". الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "ج" "أ" ولا شيء من "ج" "ر" ونتيجته إما كل "ب" "أ" ولا "ر" وإما كل "د" "أ" ولا "ر". وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات آخر ثلاثة موازية لهذه الاقترانات، فاعتبرها بنفسك. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو كليات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات ستة، إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال في النتيجة تكون جزئية. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة تألف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات اثنا عشر موازية لما سبق من الاقترانات إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك، فإنه سهل الاعتبار.
And that it be true or exclusive of emptiness without exclusiveness of union, and that the categoricals be universal, and its productive moods are twenty-four combinations, and its conclusion in all is a positive disjunctive exclusive of emptiness without exclusiveness of union. The first combination: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are universal positive and universal positive categoricals, as we say: always either every 'b' is 'j' or every 'd' is 'j', and every 'j' is 'a', and every 'j' is 'r'; its conclusion: it cannot be but either every 'b' or every 'd' is 'a' and 'r'. The second combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: either none of 'b' is 'a' and 'r' or none of 'd' is 'a' and 'r'. The third combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals differ in quality, as we say: always either every 'b' is 'j' or every 'd' is 'j', and every 'j' is 'a', and none of 'j' is 'r'; its conclusion: either every 'b' is 'a' and not 'r' or every 'd' is 'a' and not 'r'. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then other three combinations parallel to these can be composed with the categoricals, so consider them yourself. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular positive or universal negatives, then six combinations can be composed with the categoricals, except that the parts of the disjunction in the conclusion are particular. And if the disjunctive is particular positive, twelve combinations parallel to the previous combinations can be composed with the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is a particular positive disjunctive, so consider them yourself, for it is easy to consider.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage provides the conditions and enumerates the productive moods (iqiranat) for mixed syllogisms of the first figure, where the major premise is a disjunctive and the other premises are categorical (hamliyya). It specifies that the disjunctive must be 'true' (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) without exclusiveness of union (dun al-jam'), i.e., it must be a true exclusive disjunction (A or B, not both). The categoricals must be universal. There are 24 valid combinations, all yielding a universal positive disjunctive conclusion of the same type (exclusive of emptiness). The author then gives three sample combinations: first with all categoricals positive; second with all categoricals negative; third with mixed categoricals (one positive, one negative). Then he notes variations: if the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, there are three analogous combinations; if the disjunctive parts are particular positives or universal negatives, six combinations; if the disjunctive is particular positive, twelve combinations. He leaves the detailed enumeration to the reader, stating it is easy. The examples use variables (b, j, d, a, r) as placeholders for terms, typical in logical expositions.

Extending the Combinations: Quantity Variations in the Disjunctive Parts
Extension to all 24 combinations by varying disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the remaining 21 productive combinations by varying quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts and categoricals.

Core Idea
The three basic patterns multiply when the disjunctive parts vary in quantity and quality.

The author, after giving three explicit combinations, notes that if the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, there are three analogous combinations; if particular positives or universal negatives, six; and if the disjunctive is particular positive, twelve. He leaves the derivation to the reader as an exercise. This lesson will guide you through the logic of these variations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base three patterns
From the previous lesson, recall the three patterns: all positive categoricals, all negative, and mixed (one positive, one negative).
2
Consider the quantity of disjunctive parts
If the disjunctive parts are particular (partitive) instead of universal, the conclusion becomes particular (جزئية). The author indicates this when he says 'إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال في النتيجة تكون جزئية'.
3
Consider the quality of disjunctive parts
If the disjunctive parts are negative (e.g., 'some B is not J' or 'every B is not J'), the conclusion's terms may be negated. The author specifically mentions when parts are 'جزئيات سوالب' (particular negatives) or 'كليات سوالب' (universal negatives).
4
Apply analogy to count combinations
For each variation, the number of combinations multiplies: 3 base patterns * (2 possibilities for quantity? Actually, the author gives specific counts: 3 for particular negatives, 6 for particular positives or universal negatives, and 12 when the disjunctive itself is particular positive. The total sums to 24.
The author leaves the exact derivation to the reader, so this step encourages you to work out the combinations.

Second Figure Mixed Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms: Conditions and 16 Valid Moods
Author states conditions and 16 productive iqiranat for second figure mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for validity in second figure mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms and reconstruct the 16 valid moods using Arabic logical variables.

Core Idea
In second figure mixed syllogisms, categoricals must be uniform and universal; the conclusion is always an exclusive disjunction.

In this passage, the author lays out conditions for productive mixed syllogisms in the second figure. Unlike pure categorical second figure, the categorical premises must be uniform in quality (all affirmative or all negative) and universal. The author then enumerates eight specific moods (and eight more for particular disjunctives), each with an exclusive disjunctive conclusion. Understanding these rules is essential for analyzing complex arguments in Islamic logic involving disjunctive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for productivity
Read the opening line: 'وإن كانت على نسق الشكل الثاني فشرط إنتاجه...' Extract the two conditions: the disjunctive must be positive true or exclusive of emptiness without union, and the categoricals must be universal and uniform in quality.
In pure categorical second figure, premises must be of opposite quality; here they must be uniform due to the disjunctive.
2
Recognize the type of conclusion
The text says: 'والنتيجة في الكل مانعة الخلو دون الجمع.' This means the conclusion disjunction excludes only the case where both parts are false (khuluw), but allows both to be true. So it is an inclusive disjunction.
This is an exclusive-of-emptiness disjunction (inclusive 'or').
3
Trace the first combination
Work through the example: 'دائماً إما كل ب ج أو كل د ج، وكل أ ج، وكل ر ج' → The conclusion is 'دائماً إما بعض ب أو بعض د ليس أ ولا ر'. This shows the abstract pattern: disjunctive major with universal affirmative parts, two universal affirmative categoricals, conclusion: particular negative disjunctive.
Note that the categoricals share the middle term (ج) as predicate, typical of second figure.
4
Note the variation pattern across the eight combinations
The author varies the quality of the disjunctive parts (positive/negative) and the categoricals (affirmative/negative) systematically. Each combination yields a different conclusion. List the first four: (1) parts universal positive, cats positive; (2) same parts, cats negative; (3) parts universal negative, cats positive; (4) same parts, cats negative.
The fifth through eighth use particular disjunctive parts.
5
Understand the additional eight moods
The final sentence states: 'وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى ثمانية موازية... إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة.' So when the disjunctive is particular positive, there are eight parallel moods yielding particular positive conclusions.
This brings the total to 16 valid moods.

And if it is on the pattern of the second figure, then the condition for its productivity is that the disjunctive be positive true or exclusive of emptiness without exclusiveness of union, and that the categoricals be universal uniform in quality, and its productive moods are sixteen combinations, and the conclusion in all is exclusive of emptiness without exclusiveness of union. The first combination: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are universal positives and universal positive categoricals, as we say: always either every 'b' is 'j' or every 'd' is 'j', and every 'a' is 'j', and every 'r' is 'j'; its conclusion: always either some 'b' or some 'd' is not 'a' nor 'r'. The second combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: always either none of 'b' or none of 'd' is 'a' or not 'r'. The third combination: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are universal negatives and universal positive categoricals, as we say: always either none of 'b' is 'j' or none of 'd' is 'j', and every 'a' is 'j', and every 'r' is 'j'; its conclusion: always either none of 'b' is 'r' or none of 'd' is 'a' and not 'r'. The fourth combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: always either some 'b' is 'j' or some 'j' is not 'a' nor 'r'. The fifth combination: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are particular positives and universal positive categoricals, as we say: always either some 'b' is 'j' or some 'd' is 'j', and every 'a' is 'j', and every 'r' is 'j'; its conclusion: either none of 'b' is 'a' nor 'r' or none of 'd' is 'a' nor 'r'. The sixth combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: always either some 'b' or some 'd' is not 'a' nor 'r'. The seventh combination: from a universal positive disjunctive whose parts are particular negatives and universal positive categoricals, as we say: always either not every 'b' is 'j' or not every 'd' is 'j', and every 'a' is 'b', and every 'r' is 'j'; its conclusion: either some 'b' or some 'd' is not 'a' nor 'r'. The eighth combination: the combination as it is, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: either none of 'b' or none of 'd' is 'a' nor 'r'. And if the disjunctive is particular positive, then other eight combinations parallel to these can be composed with the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is a particular positive disjunctive.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage details the second figure mixed syllogisms. The conditions are: the disjunctive premise must be positive and true (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without union (i.e., exactly one part true), and the categorical premises must be universal and uniform in quality (all affirmative or all negative). There are 16 valid moods, all yielding an exclusive disjunctive conclusion (mani'at al-khuluw without jam'). The author then lists eight specific combinations (iqiranat) with their sample forms and conclusions, using schematic letters. The first four involve disjunctive parts that are universal (positive or negative) combined with categoricals that are either all positive or all negative. The fifth to eighth involve disjunctive parts that are particular (positive or negative). For each, the pattern is given with an example. Finally, he notes that if the disjunctive is particular positive, there are another eight analogous moods but with particular positive conclusions. This leaves the total as 16 (8 given + 8 analogous). The examples show that in the second figure, the middle term is predicated in both categoricals (as in standard second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises). The conclusions often involve negations.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thani): The second figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Here adapted to include a disjunctive major premise. [Note: Standard term for the second figure; no shift.]
نتيجة (natija): The conclusion of the syllogism, which here is always an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw dun al-jam') for the universal cases. [Note: Stable meaning; no shift.]
متحدة الكيفية (muttahidat al-kayfiyya): The categorical premises must be uniform in quality (all affirmative or all negative) for the syllogism to be productive in the second figure. [Note: This condition is specific to mixed syllogisms; in pure categorical second figure, the premises must have opposite quality. Here it is reversed due to the disjunctive.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second figure mixed syllogism, what is the condition on the quality of the categorical premises?
A) They must be opposite in quality
B) They must be uniform in quality
C) They must be both affirmative
D) There is no condition

Key Takeaway
In second figure mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms, the categoricals must be universal and uniform, and the conclusion is always an exclusive disjunction (inclusive 'or').

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Figure Syllogisms with Disjunctive Major
Author enumerates productive conjunctions for third figure with disjunctive major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and conditions of a third-figure syllogism with a disjunctive major and determine its categorical conclusion.

Core Idea
In the third figure, a disjunctive major with categorical minors yields a categorical conclusion.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq presents a systematic enumeration of compound syllogisms in the third figure where the major premise is a disjunction. Understanding these patterns helps you see how Islamic logicians combined different proposition types to produce certain conclusions. We will focus on one representative example to grasp the general method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise is a disjunctive (munfaṣila) that is universal affirmative, with two parts (e.g., 'always either every B is J or every D is J'). Note that the disjunctive must be real (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive of void (māniʿat al-khuluw).
2
Identify the categorical minors
There are two categorical (ḥamliyya) minor premises, each affirming a relation between one of the disjuncts and another term. For example, 'every B is A' and 'every D is R'. These must be universal affirmative in the first pattern.
The terms A, B, D, J, R are variables; their roles vary by pattern.
3
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion is always a categorical proposition, not a disjunction. In the example, it is 'some J is A and B is R' (though the exact reading may be 'some J is A and some J is R' due to OCR). The conclusion is particular affirmative or negative.
If the disjunctive is particular, the conclusion is a particular disjunctive.
4
Check the conditions
The author states conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and either real or exclusive of void (not exclusive of conjunction). Also, if the categoricals are particular, the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives; if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, then the categoricals must be universals.
These conditions ensure validity.

And if the parts of the disjunctive are universal negatives or particular affirmatives or particular negatives, then eighteen other conjunctions analogous to these conjunctions may be composed from it with the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is absolutely general, so consider all by yourself, for it is easy.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of productive conjunctions (اقترانات) for the third figure of syllogisms where the major premise is a disjunctive (منفصلة) and the minor premises are categorical (حمليات). The author first lists eight forms for a universal affirmative disjunctive with variations in the categoricals (universal positive, universal negative, particular positive, particular negative), then notes that if the disjunctive is particular, another eight analogous forms exist with particular disjunctive conclusions. Then conditions are given: the disjunctive must be affirmative and either real (حقيقية) or exclusive of void but not conjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). If the categoricals are particular, the disjunctive's parts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives; if the disjunctive's parts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, the categoricals must be universals. Then the author states there are 36 productive conjunctions for this figure, and lists three specific patterns (first, second, third) with examples, followed by additional patterns for particular disjunctive and for disjunctive parts of different quantities/qualities. The commentary should explain that each pattern consists of a disjunctive major (e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every D is J') and two categorical minors (e.g., 'Every B is A' and 'Every D is R'), yielding a categorical conclusion. For example, in fiqh: 'Always either this drink is khamr or it is nabidh, and every khamr is haram, and every nabidh is haram' would yield 'Some drink is haram' (since both are haram). The author encourages the reader to work out the full enumeration themselves.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Three Productive Conjunctions of First Figure with Universal Disjunctive
Author enumerates three specific conjunctions of categoricals with a universal affirmative disjunctive in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the three productive conjunctions of a universal affirmative disjunctive major with categorical minors in the first figure, and verify their particular categorical conclusions.

Core Idea
A universal disjunctive with two categorical minors yields a particular categorical conclusion.

In the first figure, when the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunctive and the minor premises are categoricals, three specific conjunctions produce valid conclusions. By analyzing each, you'll see how the disjunctive constrains the conclusion to a particular statement. This prepares you for more complex mixed syllogisms in legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
The major premise is always a universal affirmative disjunctive, e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every D is J'. Note that both parts of the disjunctive share the same predicate J.
The disjunctive is assumed exclusive (only one holds), though later logic refines this.
2
Identify the two categorical minor premises
The minor premises are two categorical propositions, each applying a second predicate (A and R) to the subjects B and D respectively. Their quality (affirmative/negative) varies across the three conjunctions.
In the first conjunction, both are affirmative; second, both negative; third, one negative one affirmative.
3
Determine the conclusion for each conjunction
Because the disjunctive guarantees that at least one of B or D is J, and from the minor we know that B (or D) has a certain predicate, we can infer that some J has that predicate. The conclusion is always particular (some J) because the disjunctive does not cover all J.
For example, if both minors are affirmative, conclusion is 'some J is A and B is R'. The second minor's subject D may not be J, so the conclusion about R is limited.
4
Apply the pattern to an Islamic example
Use 'Always either every khamr is intoxicating or every nabidh is intoxicating' as major. For first conjunction, minors: 'Every khamr is haram' and 'Every nabidh is permissible'. Conclusion: 'Some intoxicating thing is haram and khamr is permissible'? Wait, the conclusion links the predicates of the minors. Actually the conclusion is: 'some J is A and B is R'? The source says 'some J is A and B is R' meaning the conclusion has two parts: one about J and A, and another about B and R? But reading carefully: The source says 'some J is A and B is R' — that seems to combine two conclusions. Actually it might be two separate conclusions? The text: 'نتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ب" "ر")' meaning 'its conclusion is (some J is A and B is R)'. So the conclusion is a compound: some J is A, and every B is R. But wait, 'some J is A' comes from the first minor, and 'B is R' from the second? But B is the subject of the first minor; the second minor gives 'every D is R'. So it should be 'some J is A and every D is R'. But the text says 'B is R'. Possibly a typo? The author writes 'ب "ر"' meaning B is R, but the second minor states 'Every D is R'. There is inconsistency. For pedagogical purposes, we'll treat the conclusion as: some J is A (from the first minor) and some J is R (from the second minor) — but the source explicitly says 'B is R'. To avoid confusion, we'll simplify: The conclusion is a particular affirmative or negative about J and each predicate, but the exact form may vary. We'll stick to the source: first conjunction: some J is A and B is R. This seems to infer that B (the subject of first minor) is R from the second minor? That is invalid. Actually, the author probably means: from the second minor 'every D is R', and since the disjunctive says either B is J or D is J, if D is not J, then B is J, but we don't know if D is J. The conclusion 'some J is A' is valid from the first minor and the disjunctive. The second part 'B is R' cannot be deduced. So there may be an error in the source text. For the lesson, we'll note this complexity and focus on the structure as given, but suggest that in rigorous logic, the conclusion should be re-examined. For the guided path, we'll avoid the problematic part and focus on the general principle: the conclusion is a particular categorical proposition about J.
Due to ambiguities in the source, we recommend verifying each conjunction independently.

وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه حَمْلية: الاقتران الأول: من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليات موجبة وكليات موجبة حملية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "ب" "أ") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ب" "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب، ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "أ" ولا "ر"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" ولا شيء من "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ر") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "أ" وهو "ر").
And its productive conjunctions are thirty-six conjunctions, and its characteristic is that the conclusion in it is categorical: The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal affirmative categoricals, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and every B is A' and 'every D is R', and its conclusion is 'some J is A and B is R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and not R'. The third conjunction: the conjunction is the same except that the categoricals differ in quality, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and no B is A' and 'every B is R', and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and it is R'.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines the first three productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) of mixed syllogisms under the first figure, where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunctive (e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every D is J') and the minor premises are categoricals. The author begins by stating that there are 36 such productive conjunctions in total, and the conclusion is always categorical. The first conjunction occurs when both categoricals are universal affirmatives (e.g., 'every B is A' and 'every D is R'), yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The second conjunction is identical except the categoricals are universal negatives, producing a particular negative conclusion. The third conjunction mixes the quality: one categorical is universal negative and the other universal affirmative, resulting in a particular negative conclusion with one term affirmed. The author uses variable letters (ب, ج, د, أ, ر) to illustrate the logical forms, similar to how a faqih might use 'khamr' and 'nabidh' to test analogy.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Fourth-Figure Mixed Syllogisms with Disjunctive
Author specifies conditions for fourth figure disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the conditions required for a productive mixed syllogism in the fourth figure with a universal affirmative disjunctive.

Core Idea
The disjunctive must be inclusive (maniat al-khulu), affirmative, and categoricals cannot be particular negative.

In the fourth figure, when combining a universal disjunctive major with categorical minors, certain conditions must hold for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion. This lesson breaks down those conditions as outlined by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and type of disjunctive
The passage specifies the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi') and that the disjunctive major is universal affirmative (kulliyya mūjaba munfasila). Ensure the syllogism matches this pattern.
The disjunctive must be 'uniform in quantity and quality' (muttahidat al-kammiyya wa al-kayfiyya).
2
Check the quality of the disjunctive
The disjunctive must be affirmative (mūjaba). A negative disjunctive would not be productive under these conditions.
3
Determine the type of disjunction
The disjunctive must be either true (haqiqiyya) or inclusive (maniat al-khulu), i.e., it prevents both parts from being false but allows both to be true. It must not be exclusive (maniat al-jam').
Mani'at al-khulu is the inclusive 'or' (one or both true).
4
Examine the categorical premises
The categorical premises (hamliyyat) cannot be particular negative (juz'iyya sāliba). They can be universal affirmative, universal negative, or particular affirmative, but if they are particular affirmative, the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative.
The author says 'لا تكون الحمليّة جزئية سالبة' (the categorical must not be particular negative).
5
Verify uniformity of the disjunctive parts
The parts of the disjunctive (ajzā' al-munfasila) must be universal affirmative or particular negative, and they must agree in quantity and quality.
In the examples, the parts are universal affirmative (kulliyya mūjaba).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الرابع، فلنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية فشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن لا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت الحمليات جزئيات موجبة، وأن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والنتيجة في الكل حَمْلية: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "أ" "ب") و(كل "ر" "د") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" أو "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "ا" ولا "ر"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "أ" "ب") و(لا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "أ" ولا "ر"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحمليات جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ر").
And as for if it is in the pattern of the fourth figure, we posit the discussion when the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, so its condition for production is that the disjunctive is affirmative, and that it is true or exclusive (preventing emptiness) but not preventing conjunction, and that the categorical is not particular negative, and if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, and that the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is categorical: The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal affirmative categoricals, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and every A is B' and 'every R is D', and its conclusion is 'some J is A or R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and not R'. The third conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals differ in quality, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and every A is B' and 'nothing of R is D', and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and not R'. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are particular affirmatives, and its conclusion is 'some J is A and R'.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and enumerates four productive conjunctions for the fourth figure in mixed syllogisms. The author begins by stating the context: when the disjunctive is uniform in quantity and quality (e.g., universal affirmative), several conditions must hold: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and it must be either a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) or a maniat al-khulu (exclusive disjunction that prevents both parts from being false, but does not prevent both from being true—i.e., inclusive). Additionally, the categorical premises cannot be particular negative; they can be particular affirmative, and the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. Under these conditions, there are 24 productive conjunctions, but the author illustrates four representative ones. The first conjunction uses two universal affirmative categoricals, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The second uses universal negatives, giving a particular negative. The third mixes qualities (one universal affirmative, one universal negative), also yielding a particular negative. The fourth uses particular affirmative categoricals, giving a particular affirmative. The author uses variable letters to show the logical forms, similar to how a usuli would test the 'illa of a ruling.

مانعة الخلو
حقيقية
حمليات جزئية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT allowed in a productive fourth-figure disjunctive syllogism?
A) A disjunctive that is affirmative
B) A categorical that is particular negative
C) A disjunctive that is true (haqiqiyya)
D) A disjunctive that is maniat al-khulu

Key Takeaway
The feasibility of a mixed disjunctive syllogism in the fourth figure depends on the disjunctive being affirmative and inclusive, and the categoricals not being particular negatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Given the following: 'Always either every sale is valid or every gift is revocable. Every forward contract is a sale. Every hibah is a gift.' Is this a valid fourth-figure mixed syllogism? Check the conditions.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Applying the Four Conjunctions of the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates four productive conjunctions for fourth figure with disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the categorical conclusion for each of the four conjunctions given the premises in the fourth figure with a universal affirmative disjunctive.

Core Idea
Each conjunction produces a particular categorical conclusion based on the quality and quantity of the categorical premises.

The author enumerates four specific conjunction patterns that yield valid conclusions when the disjunctive major is universal affirmative and inclusive. This lesson walks through each pattern, showing how to apply the conditions and extract the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conjunction number
There are four conjunctions. Determine which one based on the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the categorical premises.
The author lists them in order: 1) both universal affirmative, 2) both universal negative, 3) one universal affirmative and one universal negative, 4) both particular affirmative.
2
Write the premises in variable form
Following the author's pattern, assign variables: disjunctive major: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J'; first categorical: 'every A is B' (or equivalent for other conjunctions); second categorical: 'every R is D' (or equivalent).
In the fourth conjunction, the categoricals are particular affirmative: 'some A is B' and 'some R is D'.
3
Apply the rule for the conclusion
For the first conjunction, the conclusion is 'some J is A or R'. For the second, 'some J is not A and not R'. For the third, 'some J is not A and not R' (same as second). For the fourth, 'some J is A and R'.
Note the difference: in the first and fourth, the predicate is positive; in the second and third, negative. Also, the connective changes from 'or' to 'and' between first and fourth.
4
Verify the conclusion's quantity and quality
All conclusions are particular (juz'iyya). The quality matches the conjunction: affirmative for first and fourth, negative for second and third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الرابع، فلنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية فشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن لا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت الحمليات جزئيات موجبة، وأن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والنتيجة في الكل حَمْلية: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "أ" "ب") و(كل "ر" "د") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" أو "ر"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "ا" ولا "ر"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "أ" "ب") و(لا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" ليس "أ" ولا "ر"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحمليات جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ر").
And as for if it is in the pattern of the fourth figure, we posit the discussion when the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, so its condition for production is that the disjunctive is affirmative, and that it is true or exclusive (preventing emptiness) but not preventing conjunction, and that the categorical is not particular negative, and if the categoricals are particular affirmatives, and that the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is categorical: The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal affirmative categoricals, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and every A is B' and 'every R is D', and its conclusion is 'some J is A or R'. The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and not R'. The third conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals differ in quality, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J, and every A is B' and 'nothing of R is D', and its conclusion is 'some J is not A and not R'. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the categoricals are particular affirmatives, and its conclusion is 'some J is A and R'.
